Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Al-Hajiyyat: Needs in Islamic Law
Explains the second category of masalih: al-hajiyyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-hajiyyat, provide examples such as ijarah, and distinguish them from daruriyyat.

Core Idea
Hajiyyat remove hardship without threatening the five essentials.

In the previous lesson, we examined the daruriyyat (essentials) that safeguard the five fundamental interests. Now we turn to the second category: al-hajiyyat (needs). These are actions and transactions that are not critical for survival but are necessary to ease life and avoid undue hardship. The author uses the contract of ijarah (leasing) as a prime example, illustrating how Islamic law facilitates transactions to meet societal needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Read the text's definition of hajiyyat: actions and transactions not essential for the five fundamentals but required for expansion and removal of hardship.
Key phrase: 'لتوسعة ورفع الحرج'.
2
Understand the example of ijarah
Analyze why ijarah is a hajiyya: it allows temporary use of property without ownership, fulfilling a need without disrupting life.
Note the contrast: if ijarah were blocked, life would not be corrupted but hardship would occur.
3
Distinguish from daruriyyat
Hajiyyat differ from daruriyyat: daruriyyat are essential for the five pillars; hajiyyat are not, but their absence causes significant difficulty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٥) المصادر التبعية الاستصلاح

الثاني: الحاجيات. وهي الأعمال والتصرّفات التي لا تتوقف عليها صيانة تلك الأركان الخمسة، ولكنها تتطلبها الحاجة لأجل التوسعة ورفع الحرج كإباحة الصيد والتمتع بالطيبات التي يمكن أن يستغني عنها الإنسان ولكن بشيء من المشقة؛ وكتشريع عقد الاستئجار وكثير من أنواع المعاملات .

ففتح طريق الاستئجار في المعاملات المدنية يحقق للناس وفاء حاجة كبرى، إذ يتمكن به كل منهم أن ينتفع بملك غيره لقاء عوض يسير بالنسبة إلى قيمة عين الشيء المأجور. ولو سُدَّ طريق الاستئجار لما تعطلت حياة الناس وما فسدت أوضاعهم، ولكن ينالهم حرج كبير : إذ يضطر الإنسان أن يملك عين ما يحتاج إليه ولو حاجة موقتة .

الثالث: التكميليات أو التحسينيات وهي التي لا تتحرج الحياة بتركها ولكن مراعاتها من مكارم الأخلاق أو من محاسن العادات، فهي من قبيل استكمال ما يليق والتنزه عما لا يليق، كآداب الكلام والطعام والشراب وكالاعتدال في المظاهر والاقتصاد في المصارف دون إسراف ولا تقتير ونحو ذلك.

وعلى هذا فالأحكام التي شرعت لصيانة الأركان الضرورية هي أهم الأحكام وأحقها بالمراعاة وتليها الأحكام المشروعة لضمان الحاجيات، ثم الأحكام المشروعة للتحسين والتكميل.

ولا يراعى حكم تحسيني إذا كان في مراعاته إخلال بما هو ضروري أو حاجي، لأن الفرع لا يراعى إذا كان في مراعاته والمحافظة عليه تفريط في الأصل ولذلك أبيح شرعاً كشف العورة عند الاقتضاء لأجل تشخيص داء، أو لمداواة أو عملية جراحية ضرورية، لأن ستر العورة من الأمور التحسينية التي غايتها أدبية ؛ أما العلاج فمن الضروريات لأن به صيانة النفس أو العقل أو النسل.

(ر: كتاب أصول الفقه للأستاذ الجليل الشيخ عبد الوهاب الخلاف ص / ١٦٣ الطبعة الثانية و المدخل إلى علم أصول الفقه» للزميل الأستاذ المحقق الدكتور معروف الدواليبي ص/ ٤١٦ - ٤١٧ الطبعة الثانية) .

هذه هي الأسس التي يعتبرها الشرع الإسلامي في وزن المصالح المرسلة أنواعاً ودرجات وهي ترسم مقاصد الشريعة كما تدل عليه دلائل نصوصها في شتى الموضوعات والقضايا والأحكام.

فكل ما يؤيد هذه المقاصد الشرعية ويساعد على تحقيقها فهو مصلحة مطلوبة طلباً قوياً أو ضعيفاً بحسب موقعها في تلك الأقسام الثلاثة. وكل ما ينافيها فهو مفسدة ممنوعة منعاً شديداً أو ضعيفاً بحسب نوع المقصد الشرعي الذي تخل به (۱).

ويتضح مما تقدم أن التحسينيات منها ما هو من المندوبات أي : المطلوبة طلباً خفيفاً على سبيل الأولوية والأفضلية كآداب الطعام ونحوها، ومنها ما هو من الفرائض أي المطلوبة شرعاً على سبيل الحتم والإيجاب كستر العورة؛ لأن معنى كون الشيء من التحسينيات هو أن الناس يمكنهم الاستغناء عنه في حياتهم المادية دون حرج، ولكنه قد يكون مما تقتضي الاعتبارات الأدبية والمعنوية تحتيمه وإلزام الناس به.

٥/ ٥ - كل أمر فيه جهتا نفع وضرر ؛ والعبرة شرعاً للغالب :

هذا، ومن المسلم به أن كل أمر من الأمور فيه جهة نفع وجهة ضرر متعادلتان أو متفاوتتان فإذا كانت جهة النفع في الشيء هي الغالبة فهو

(۱) أوضح الأمام الغزالي في كتابه المستصفى في أصول الفقه أسس هذه المصالح المرسلة وأقسامها الثلاثة : الضروريات الخمس والحاجيات، والتحسينيات). ثم جاء الإمام أبو إسحاق الشاطبي المالكي فأفاض فيها تبياناً وتعليلاً وتحليلاً بما لم يسبق إليه في كتابه الموافقات في أصول الشريعة

ثم تناولها في هذا العصر يبحث ماتع الأستاذ الشيخ محمد أبو زهرة بمصر في كتابه «مالك». كما تناولها بمعالجة مبتكرة مع إضافات قيمة الشيخ الجليل محمد الطاهر ابن عاشور علامة تونس في كتابه مقاصد الشريعة الإسلامية تونس، طبعة أولى، ١٣٦٦ هـ).
Chapter (5): Subsidiary Sources - Istislah

Second: al-Hajiyyat (the needs). They are actions and transactions that are not essential for safeguarding those five fundamentals, but need requires them for expansion and removal of hardship, such as permitting hunting and enjoying good things that a person could do without but with some difficulty; and like legislation of the contract of ijarah and many types of transactions.

Opening the way of ijarah in civil transactions fulfills a great need for people, as each can benefit from another's property for a small compensation relative to the value of the leased item. If the way of ijarah were blocked, people's lives would not be disrupted nor their situations corrupted, but great hardship would befall them: a person would be forced to own the very thing he needs, even if for a temporary need.

Third: al-Takmiliyyat or al-Tahsiniyyat (the complementary/embellishments). They are those matters that life is not distressed by abandoning them, but observing them is from noble morals or good habits. They are of the type of completing what is fitting and keeping away from what is not fitting, like etiquette of speech, food, drink, moderation in appearance, economy in spending without extravagance or stinginess, and the like.

Thus, the rulings legislated to safeguard the essential pillars are the most important rulings and most deserving of consideration, followed by rulings legislated to guarantee the needs, then rulings legislated for improvement and completion.

A tahsini ruling is not observed if its observance causes harm to what is essential or necessary, because the subsidiary is not observed if its observance and preservation cause neglect of the principle. Therefore, it is legally permitted to uncover the private parts when necessary for diagnosis, treatment, or a necessary surgical operation, because covering the private parts is from the tahsini matters whose purpose is ethical; whereas treatment is from the necessities because it preserves life, intellect, or lineage.

(Ref: Book Usul al-Fiqh by the venerable professor Sheikh Abd al-Wahhab Khallaf p. 163, second edition; and al-Madkhal ila 'Ilm Usul al-Fiqh by the colleague professor investigator Dr. Ma'ruf al-Dawalibi pp. 416-417, second edition.)

These are the foundations that Islamic law considers in weighing masalih mursalah, types and degrees, and they outline the objectives of the Shari'ah as indicated by the evidence of its texts across various topics, issues, and rulings.

So everything that supports these Shari'ah objectives and helps achieve them is a maslaha (benefit) sought strongly or weakly according to its position in those three categories. And everything that contradicts them is a mafsada (harm) prohibited severely or weakly according to the type of Shari'ah objective it violates (1).

It becomes clear from the above that tahsiniyyat include what is mandub (recommended) - that is, sought lightly by way of preference and virtue, like etiquette of food and the like - and include what is fara'id (obligatory) - that is, required legally by way of determination and obligation, like covering the private parts. Because the meaning of something being from tahsiniyyat is that people can dispense with it in their material lives without hardship, but it may be that ethical and moral considerations require imposing it and obligating people to it.

5/5 - Every matter has two aspects: benefit and harm; and legal consideration is for the predominant.

This is, and it is well-known that every matter has a benefit aspect and a harm aspect, equal or unequal. So if the benefit aspect in a thing is predominant, then it is...

(1) Imam al-Ghazali explained in his work al-Mustasfa fi Usul al-Fiqh the foundations of these masalih mursalah and their three divisions: the five essentials, needs, and embellishments. Then came Imam Abu Ishaq al-Shatibi al-Maliki and elaborated on them in explanation, reasoning, and analysis as never before in his work al-Muwafaqat fi Usul al-Shari'ah.

Then in this era, they were taken up in a delightful study by Professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra in his book 'Malik'. They were also taken up with innovative treatment and valuable additions by the venerable Sheikh Muhammad al-Tahir ibn Ashur, the scholar of Tunis, in his book Maqasid al-Shari'ah al-Islamiyyah, Tunis, first edition, 1366 AH.)
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm explains the subsidiary source of istislah (public interest) by detailing the three-tier classification of masalih (interests) that Islamic law seeks to protect. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, follows the framework of Imam al-Ghazali and al-Shatibi, dividing masalih into daruriyyat (essentials), hajiyyat (needs), and tahsiniyyat (embellishments). The daruriyyat are the five essentials: religion, life, intellect, lineage, and property, though these are mentioned earlier; here the focus is on the other two. Hajiyyat are actions and transactions that remove hardship and provide ease, such as the permissibility of hunting for enjoyment and the legislation of ijarah (leasing). Ijarah is highlighted as a prime example because it allows people to benefit from others' property for a temporary period without the burden of ownership, fulfilling a major need without causing disruption. Tahsiniyyat are matters of moral and ethical perfection, like table manners and moderation in spending. The text emphasizes a hierarchy: daruriyyat take precedence over hajiyyat, and hajiyyat over tahsiniyyat. A tahsini ruling must yield if it conflicts with a higher category, illustrated by the example of uncovering the private parts for medical treatment—covering the private parts is a tahsini obligation, but treatment is a daruri necessity, so the latter prevails. The passage then clarifies that tahsiniyyat are not limited to recommended acts; they can also be obligatory, as in covering the private parts (which is fard), because the defining characteristic of tahsiniyyat is that one can forgo them in material life without distress, but moral and ethical considerations may make them required. Finally, the text introduces a foundational rule for weighing masalih: every matter has benefit and harm, and the predominant side determines the ruling. This principle is essential for applying istislah, as it allows scholars to evaluate whether an action serves the Shari'ah objectives overall. The passage concludes with references to classical and contemporary works on usul al-fiqh and maqasid al-shari'ah.

Key Terms
الحاجيات (al-hajiyyat): Needs: actions and transactions required to remove hardship and facilitate life, but not essential for the five fundamentals.
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): A subsidiary source of fiqh where a ruling is based on considering public interest (maslaha) that is not explicitly regulated by primary texts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a hajiyyat according to the text?
A) Preserving one's life
B) The contract of ijarah (leasing)
C) Etiquette of eating
D) Covering the private parts

Key Takeaway
Hajiyyat are needs that Islamic law facilitates to prevent hardship, but they remain subordinate to daruriyyat.

Al-Tahsiniyyat: Embellishments and Their Legal Scope
Explains the third category of masalih: al-tahsiniyyat and their dual nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tahsiniyyat, distinguish between their mandub and fard subcategories, and apply the rule that tahsiniyyat yield to higher interests.

Core Idea
Tahsiniyyat enhance moral character, but they can be obligatory and must give way to daruriyyat and hajiyyat.

Having understood daruriyyat and hajiyyat, we now explore the third and lowest tier: al-tahsiniyyat (embellishments). These are matters that life can endure without, but their observance reflects noble morals and good habits. The text makes a crucial point: tahsiniyyat can be either recommended (mandub) or obligatory (fard), as in the case of covering the private parts. This lesson clarifies the scope of tahsiniyyat and their hierarchical subordination.

Guided Path
1
Define tahsiniyyat
Read the text's definition: matters not causing hardship if abandoned, but observed for moral and ethical perfection.
Examples: etiquette of speech, food, drink, moderation.
2
Distinguish between mandub and fard tahsiniyyat
Note that tahsiniyyat include both recommended acts (adab al-ta'am) and obligatory acts (covering awrah). The defining feature is that they can be foregone without material hardship.
Covering awrah is fard but still tahsini because one can live without it materially, though it is ethically required.
3
Apply the priority rule
A tahsini ruling is not observed if it conflicts with a daruri or haji ruling. Example: uncovering awrah for medical treatment is allowed.
This illustrates the principle that the subsidiary yields to the primary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٥) المصادر التبعية الاستصلاح

الثاني: الحاجيات. وهي الأعمال والتصرّفات التي لا تتوقف عليها صيانة تلك الأركان الخمسة، ولكنها تتطلبها الحاجة لأجل التوسعة ورفع الحرج كإباحة الصيد والتمتع بالطيبات التي يمكن أن يستغني عنها الإنسان ولكن بشيء من المشقة؛ وكتشريع عقد الاستئجار وكثير من أنواع المعاملات .

ففتح طريق الاستئجار في المعاملات المدنية يحقق للناس وفاء حاجة كبرى، إذ يتمكن به كل منهم أن ينتفع بملك غيره لقاء عوض يسير بالنسبة إلى قيمة عين الشيء المأجور. ولو سُدَّ طريق الاستئجار لما تعطلت حياة الناس وما فسدت أوضاعهم، ولكن ينالهم حرج كبير : إذ يضطر الإنسان أن يملك عين ما يحتاج إليه ولو حاجة موقتة .

الثالث: التكميليات أو التحسينيات وهي التي لا تتحرج الحياة بتركها ولكن مراعاتها من مكارم الأخلاق أو من محاسن العادات، فهي من قبيل استكمال ما يليق والتنزه عما لا يليق، كآداب الكلام والطعام والشراب وكالاعتدال في المظاهر والاقتصاد في المصارف دون إسراف ولا تقتير ونحو ذلك.

وعلى هذا فالأحكام التي شرعت لصيانة الأركان الضرورية هي أهم الأحكام وأحقها بالمراعاة وتليها الأحكام المشروعة لضمان الحاجيات، ثم الأحكام المشروعة للتحسين والتكميل.

ولا يراعى حكم تحسيني إذا كان في مراعاته إخلال بما هو ضروري أو حاجي، لأن الفرع لا يراعى إذا كان في مراعاته والمحافظة عليه تفريط في الأصل ولذلك أبيح شرعاً كشف العورة عند الاقتضاء لأجل تشخيص داء، أو لمداواة أو عملية جراحية ضرورية، لأن ستر العورة من الأمور التحسينية التي غايتها أدبية ؛ أما العلاج فمن الضروريات لأن به صيانة النفس أو العقل أو النسل.

(ر: كتاب أصول الفقه للأستاذ الجليل الشيخ عبد الوهاب الخلاف ص / ١٦٣ الطبعة الثانية و المدخل إلى علم أصول الفقه» للزميل الأستاذ المحقق الدكتور معروف الدواليبي ص/ ٤١٦ - ٤١٧ الطبعة الثانية) .

هذه هي الأسس التي يعتبرها الشرع الإسلامي في وزن المصالح المرسلة أنواعاً ودرجات وهي ترسم مقاصد الشريعة كما تدل عليه دلائل نصوصها في شتى الموضوعات والقضايا والأحكام.

فكل ما يؤيد هذه المقاصد الشرعية ويساعد على تحقيقها فهو مصلحة مطلوبة طلباً قوياً أو ضعيفاً بحسب موقعها في تلك الأقسام الثلاثة. وكل ما ينافيها فهو مفسدة ممنوعة منعاً شديداً أو ضعيفاً بحسب نوع المقصد الشرعي الذي تخل به (۱).

ويتضح مما تقدم أن التحسينيات منها ما هو من المندوبات أي : المطلوبة طلباً خفيفاً على سبيل الأولوية والأفضلية كآداب الطعام ونحوها، ومنها ما هو من الفرائض أي المطلوبة شرعاً على سبيل الحتم والإيجاب كستر العورة؛ لأن معنى كون الشيء من التحسينيات هو أن الناس يمكنهم الاستغناء عنه في حياتهم المادية دون حرج، ولكنه قد يكون مما تقتضي الاعتبارات الأدبية والمعنوية تحتيمه وإلزام الناس به.

٥/ ٥ - كل أمر فيه جهتا نفع وضرر ؛ والعبرة شرعاً للغالب :

هذا، ومن المسلم به أن كل أمر من الأمور فيه جهة نفع وجهة ضرر متعادلتان أو متفاوتتان فإذا كانت جهة النفع في الشيء هي الغالبة فهو

(۱) أوضح الأمام الغزالي في كتابه المستصفى في أصول الفقه أسس هذه المصالح المرسلة وأقسامها الثلاثة : الضروريات الخمس والحاجيات، والتحسينيات). ثم جاء الإمام أبو إسحاق الشاطبي المالكي فأفاض فيها تبياناً وتعليلاً وتحليلاً بما لم يسبق إليه في كتابه الموافقات في أصول الشريعة

ثم تناولها في هذا العصر يبحث ماتع الأستاذ الشيخ محمد أبو زهرة بمصر في كتابه «مالك». كما تناولها بمعالجة مبتكرة مع إضافات قيمة الشيخ الجليل محمد الطاهر ابن عاشور علامة تونس في كتابه مقاصد الشريعة الإسلامية تونس، طبعة أولى، ١٣٦٦ هـ).
Chapter (5): Subsidiary Sources - Istislah

Second: al-Hajiyyat (the needs). They are actions and transactions that are not essential for safeguarding those five fundamentals, but need requires them for expansion and removal of hardship, such as permitting hunting and enjoying good things that a person could do without but with some difficulty; and like legislation of the contract of ijarah and many types of transactions.

Opening the way of ijarah in civil transactions fulfills a great need for people, as each can benefit from another's property for a small compensation relative to the value of the leased item. If the way of ijarah were blocked, people's lives would not be disrupted nor their situations corrupted, but great hardship would befall them: a person would be forced to own the very thing he needs, even if for a temporary need.

Third: al-Takmiliyyat or al-Tahsiniyyat (the complementary/embellishments). They are those matters that life is not distressed by abandoning them, but observing them is from noble morals or good habits. They are of the type of completing what is fitting and keeping away from what is not fitting, like etiquette of speech, food, drink, moderation in appearance, economy in spending without extravagance or stinginess, and the like.

Thus, the rulings legislated to safeguard the essential pillars are the most important rulings and most deserving of consideration, followed by rulings legislated to guarantee the needs, then rulings legislated for improvement and completion.

A tahsini ruling is not observed if its observance causes harm to what is essential or necessary, because the subsidiary is not observed if its observance and preservation cause neglect of the principle. Therefore, it is legally permitted to uncover the private parts when necessary for diagnosis, treatment, or a necessary surgical operation, because covering the private parts is from the tahsini matters whose purpose is ethical; whereas treatment is from the necessities because it preserves life, intellect, or lineage.

(Ref: Book Usul al-Fiqh by the venerable professor Sheikh Abd al-Wahhab Khallaf p. 163, second edition; and al-Madkhal ila 'Ilm Usul al-Fiqh by the colleague professor investigator Dr. Ma'ruf al-Dawalibi pp. 416-417, second edition.)

These are the foundations that Islamic law considers in weighing masalih mursalah, types and degrees, and they outline the objectives of the Shari'ah as indicated by the evidence of its texts across various topics, issues, and rulings.

So everything that supports these Shari'ah objectives and helps achieve them is a maslaha (benefit) sought strongly or weakly according to its position in those three categories. And everything that contradicts them is a mafsada (harm) prohibited severely or weakly according to the type of Shari'ah objective it violates (1).

It becomes clear from the above that tahsiniyyat include what is mandub (recommended) - that is, sought lightly by way of preference and virtue, like etiquette of food and the like - and include what is fara'id (obligatory) - that is, required legally by way of determination and obligation, like covering the private parts. Because the meaning of something being from tahsiniyyat is that people can dispense with it in their material lives without hardship, but it may be that ethical and moral considerations require imposing it and obligating people to it.

5/5 - Every matter has two aspects: benefit and harm; and legal consideration is for the predominant.

This is, and it is well-known that every matter has a benefit aspect and a harm aspect, equal or unequal. So if the benefit aspect in a thing is predominant, then it is...

(1) Imam al-Ghazali explained in his work al-Mustasfa fi Usul al-Fiqh the foundations of these masalih mursalah and their three divisions: the five essentials, needs, and embellishments. Then came Imam Abu Ishaq al-Shatibi al-Maliki and elaborated on them in explanation, reasoning, and analysis as never before in his work al-Muwafaqat fi Usul al-Shari'ah.

Then in this era, they were taken up in a delightful study by Professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra in his book 'Malik'. They were also taken up with innovative treatment and valuable additions by the venerable Sheikh Muhammad al-Tahir ibn Ashur, the scholar of Tunis, in his book Maqasid al-Shari'ah al-Islamiyyah, Tunis, first edition, 1366 AH.)
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm explains the subsidiary source of istislah (public interest) by detailing the three-tier classification of masalih (interests) that Islamic law seeks to protect. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, follows the framework of Imam al-Ghazali and al-Shatibi, dividing masalih into daruriyyat (essentials), hajiyyat (needs), and tahsiniyyat (embellishments). The daruriyyat are the five essentials: religion, life, intellect, lineage, and property, though these are mentioned earlier; here the focus is on the other two. Hajiyyat are actions and transactions that remove hardship and provide ease, such as the permissibility of hunting for enjoyment and the legislation of ijarah (leasing). Ijarah is highlighted as a prime example because it allows people to benefit from others' property for a temporary period without the burden of ownership, fulfilling a major need without causing disruption. Tahsiniyyat are matters of moral and ethical perfection, like table manners and moderation in spending. The text emphasizes a hierarchy: daruriyyat take precedence over hajiyyat, and hajiyyat over tahsiniyyat. A tahsini ruling must yield if it conflicts with a higher category, illustrated by the example of uncovering the private parts for medical treatment—covering the private parts is a tahsini obligation, but treatment is a daruri necessity, so the latter prevails. The passage then clarifies that tahsiniyyat are not limited to recommended acts; they can also be obligatory, as in covering the private parts (which is fard), because the defining characteristic of tahsiniyyat is that one can forgo them in material life without distress, but moral and ethical considerations may make them required. Finally, the text introduces a foundational rule for weighing masalih: every matter has benefit and harm, and the predominant side determines the ruling. This principle is essential for applying istislah, as it allows scholars to evaluate whether an action serves the Shari'ah objectives overall. The passage concludes with references to classical and contemporary works on usul al-fiqh and maqasid al-shari'ah.

Key Terms
التحسينيات (al-tahsiniyyat): Complementary matters that improve moral and social conduct; they can be obligatory if required by moral considerations, but are called tahsini because they are not vital for material existence.
التكميليات (al-takmiliyyat): Synonym for tahsiniyyat, emphasizing their complementary nature.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a tahsiniyyat that is also fard?
A) Using miswak
B) Covering the private parts
C) Giving charity
D) Smiling at others

Key Takeaway
Tahsiniyyat are not merely recommended; they can be obligatory, but they always yield to higher interests.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
The Principle of Predominance: Al-Ghalib in Balancing Benefits and Harms
Introduces the qawida of predominance as a tool for maslahah balancing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the qawida 'al-ibra li al-ghalib', apply it to cases with mixed benefit and harm, and recognize its role in istislah.

Core Idea
When benefit and harm coexist, the predominant side determines the ruling.

The classification of masalih into three tiers provides a framework for prioritizing interests. But how do we rule when a single action has both benefit and harm? The text introduces a key principle: 'Every matter has two aspects: benefit and harm; and legal consideration is for the predominant.' This qawida is essential for applying istislah and resolving conflicts of interest. In this lesson, we examine this rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida
The text asserts: 'كل أمر فيه جهتا نفع وضرر؛ والعبرة شرعاً للغالب'. Every matter has benefit and harm; consideration is for the predominant.
This is a broad principle applicable across fiqh.
2
Understand the condition of predominance
The rule applies when benefit and harm are not equal; the predominant side is decisive.
If equal, other considerations may apply (e.g., preferring the safer side).
3
Apply to the medical example
Treating a disease (benefit) may require uncovering awrah (harm). The benefit of preserving life is predominant, so the action is permissible.
This demonstrates how the qawida works with the hierarchy of masalih.

Source Text

Chapter (5): Subsidiary Sources - Istislah

Second: al-Hajiyyat (the needs). They are actions and transactions that are not essential for safeguarding those five fundamentals, but need requires them for expansion and removal of hardship, such as permitting hunting and enjoying good things that a person could do without but with some difficulty; and like legislation of the contract of ijarah and many types of transactions.

And everything that contradicts them is a mafsada (harm) prohibited severely or weakly according to the type of Shari'ah objective it violates (1).

This is, and it is well-known that every matter has a benefit aspect and a harm aspect, equal or unequal. So if the benefit aspect in a thing is predominant, then it is...

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm explains the subsidiary source of istislah (public interest) by detailing the three-tier classification of masalih (interests) that Islamic law seeks to protect. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, follows the framework of Imam al-Ghazali and al-Shatibi, dividing masalih into daruriyyat (essentials), hajiyyat (needs), and tahsiniyyat (embellishments). The daruriyyat are the five essentials: religion, life, intellect, lineage, and property, though these are mentioned earlier; here the focus is on the other two. Hajiyyat are actions and transactions that remove hardship and provide ease, such as the permissibility of hunting for enjoyment and the legislation of ijarah (leasing). Ijarah is highlighted as a prime example because it allows people to benefit from others' property for a temporary period without the burden of ownership, fulfilling a major need without causing disruption. Tahsiniyyat are matters of moral and ethical perfection, like table manners and moderation in spending. The text emphasizes a hierarchy: daruriyyat take precedence over hajiyyat, and hajiyyat over tahsiniyyat. A tahsini ruling must yield if it conflicts with a higher category, illustrated by the example of uncovering the private parts for medical treatment—covering the private parts is a tahsini obligation, but treatment is a daruri necessity, so the latter prevails. The passage then clarifies that tahsiniyyat are not limited to recommended acts; they can also be obligatory, as in covering the private parts (which is fard), because the defining characteristic of tahsiniyyat is that one can forgo them in material life without distress, but moral and ethical considerations may make them required. Finally, the text introduces a foundational rule for weighing masalih: every matter has benefit and harm, and the predominant side determines the ruling. This principle is essential for applying istislah, as it allows scholars to evaluate whether an action serves the Shari'ah objectives overall. The passage concludes with references to classical and contemporary works on usul al-fiqh and maqasid al-shari'ah.

Key Terms
العبرة للغالب (al-ibra li al-ghalib): Legal consideration is for the predominant aspect; used when weighing benefits and harms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling when an action has equal benefit and harm according to the qawida presented?
A) Permissible because benefit exists.
B) Prohibited because harm is not less.
C) The qawida does not specify; other principles apply.
D) It becomes obligatory to avoid the action.

Key Takeaway
The qawida 'al-ibra li al-ghalib' is a key tool for istislah but must be applied within the maqasid hierarchy.

The Three Categories of Maslahah: Daruriyyat, Hajiyyat, Tahsiniyyat
Explains the three levels of maslahah and their Shari'ah significance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three categories of maslahah to classify a given Shari'ah ruling and explain why tahsiniyyat can sometimes be obligatory.

Core Idea
Maslahah is graded: daruriyyat are essential, hajiyyat remove hardship, and tahsiniyyat perfect conduct—even becoming obligatory if ethics demand.

Islamic law does not treat all benefits equally. It weighs them according to their importance in achieving the objectives of Shari'ah. This lesson introduces the three-tier hierarchy of maslahah—necessities, needs, and improvements—that every mufti must understand when applying maslahah mursalah. The passage also highlights that tahsiniyyat can be either recommended or obligatory, depending on moral considerations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The text lists daruriyyat, hajiyyat, and tahsiniyyat. Recognise each as a distinct level of maslahah with different strength of demand.
Daruriyyat are the five essentials; hajiyyat are needs that remove hardship; tahsiniyyat are improvements for ethical perfection.
2
Understand the weighing principle
The text states that every act supporting the maqasid is a maslahah sought strongly or weakly according to its category. Every act contradicting them is a mafsadah prohibited proportionally.
This is the operative principle for a mufti: the ruling's strength correlates with the maslahah level.
3
Analyse the subcategories of tahsiniyyat
The text clarifies that tahsiniyyat include both mandub (recommended) and fard (obligatory) acts. Covering the 'awrah is given as an example of an obligatory tahsini.
This shows that 'improvement' is not just optional; moral necessity can make it binding.
4
Trace the scholarly development
The footnote credits al-Ghazali with first systematising these categories, al-Shatibi with expanding them, and modern scholars like Abu Zahrah and Ibn 'Ashur with further development.
This chain shows the evolution of maqasid theory, important for understanding its authoritative weight.

Chapter Two: Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence
(Fiqh) by the respected investigative professor Dr. Ma'ruf al-Dawalibi, pp. 416-417 (second edition).
These are the foundations that Islamic law considers in weighing the types and degrees of unrestricted interests (masalih mursalah), and they delineate the objectives of the Shari'ah as indicated by the evidences of its texts in various subjects, issues, and rulings.
So everything that supports these Shari'ah objectives and helps achieve them is a benefit (maslahah) sought strongly or weakly according to its position in those three divisions. And everything that contradicts them is a harm (mafsadah) prohibited strongly or weakly according to the type of Shari'ah objective it violates (1).
It becomes clear from the above that among the improvements (tahsiniyyat) there are those that are recommended (mandubat): that is, sought lightly by way of preference and superiority, like etiquette of eating and the like; and among them there are those that are obligations (fara'id): that is, required by law categorically and obligatorily, like covering the 'awrah; because the meaning of something being an improvement is that people can dispense with it in their material lives without hardship, but ethical and moral considerations may necessitate it and impose it upon people.
(1) Imam al-Ghazali clarified in his book al-Mustasfa fi Usul al-Fiqh the foundations of these unrestricted interests and their three divisions: the five necessities (daruriyyat), needs (hajiyyat), and improvements (tahsiniyyat). Then Imam Abu Ishaq al-Shatibi al-Maliki came and elaborated on them in explanation, justification, and analysis in a way not done before in his book al-Muwafaqat fi Usul al-Shari'ah. Then in this era, the respected scholar Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahrah in Egypt dealt with them in his book 'Malik'. Likewise, the venerable Sheikh Muhammad al-Tahir ibn 'Ashur, the scholar of Tunisia, dealt with them with innovative treatment and valuable additions in his book Maqasid al-Shari'ah al-Islamiyyah, Tunisia, first edition, 1366 AH).
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage introduces the three categories of maslahah (benefit) in the context of maslahah mursalah (unrestricted public interest). The author states that Islamic law weighs masalih according to their types and degrees, which correspond to the maqasid (objectives) of Shari'ah. Any action that supports these objectives is a maslahah, and any that contradicts them is a mafsadah. The strength of the demand or prohibition varies with the category. The three categories are: daruriyyat (necessities), hajiyyat (needs), and tahsiniyyat (improvements). The text specifically elaborates on tahsiniyyat, noting that they include both recommended acts (mandubat) and obligatory acts (fara'id) if the ethical or moral consideration necessitates them, even though they are not essential for material life. The footnote credits al-Ghazali for first systematizing these categories, al-Shatibi for expanding them, and modern scholars Muhammad Abu Zahrah and Muhammad al-Tahir ibn 'Ashur for further development.

Key Terms
المصلحة (al-maslahah): A benefit or interest that the Shari'ah seeks to achieve; anything that supports the objectives of Shari'ah.
الضروريات (al-daruriyyat): Necessities: the five essentials (religion, life, intellect, lineage, property) whose preservation is obligatory.
الحاجيات (al-hajiyyat): Needs: matters that remove hardship but are not essential for survival.
التحسينيات (al-tahsiniyyat): Improvements: commendable acts that perfect conduct and ethics; may be mandub or fard if required by moral considerations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are always recommendable (mandub) and never obligatory.
B) They can sometimes be obligatory if ethical considerations demand it.
C) They are essential for the preservation of the five necessities.
D) They are always forbidden because they go beyond basic needs.

Key Takeaway
The three categories of maslahah—daruriyyat, hajiyyat, tahsiniyyat—form the foundational framework for weighing benefits in Islamic law, and tahsiniyyat can rise to obligatory status when ethical necessity demands it.

Al-Istislah (Maslahah Mursalah): Definition and Factors
Defining maslaha/mafsada and listing four factors for istislah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define maslaha and mafsada in the customary sense, and identify the four factors that necessitate resorting to istislah in deriving new rulings.

Core Idea
Maslaha is predominant benefit; mafsada predominant harm; istislah serves Shari'ah objectives, not desires.

In this lesson, we explore al-istislah (consideration of public interest) as a secondary source of Islamic jurisprudence. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, defines the key terms maslaha and mafsada based on preponderance, emphasizes that the Lawgiver overlooks subjugated aspects, and then outlines four factors that push a jurist or ruler to use istislah. Understanding these factors is crucial for deriving rulings in new situations while staying true to the objectives of the Shari'ah.

Guided Path
1
Define maslaha and mafsada
Read the definitions carefully. Maslaha is benefit where the beneficial aspect predominates, even if some harm exists. Mafsada is harm where the harmful aspect predominates, even if some benefit exists. Note that the subjugated aspect is overlooked.
Focus on the concept of preponderance (ghalaba), not absolute purity.
2
Understand the principle of preponderance
The passage states that acts are deemed permissible or prohibited based on the preponderant aspect, not on the existence of any minor benefit or harm. This is a key principle in weighing maslaha and mafsada.
3
Recognize Shari'ah objectives
The benefits considered by the Law are not based on personal desires but on establishing worldly life as a bridge to the Hereafter. This aligns with maqasid al-Shari'ah (higher objectives).
Imam al-Shatibi's quote reinforces this point.
4
List the four factors for istislah
The passage identifies four factors: (1) bringing benefits (jalb al-masalih), (2) repelling harms (dar' al-mafasid), (3) blocking means (sadd al-dhara'i'), and (4) change of time (taghayyur al-zaman). Understand each factor and its examples.
These factors are not mutually exclusive; they often overlap.
5
Apply to a modern scenario
Consider a modern issue like imposing fair taxes for public services. This falls under 'bringing benefits' and 'repelling harms'. Use the four-factor framework to see how istislah operates.
The Hanafi school typically incorporates such reasoning through istihsan.

الفصل (٥) المصادر التبعية : الاستصلاح
مصلحة بالمعنى العرفي وإن اشتمل على ضرر مغلوب. وإذا كانت جهة الضرر هي الغالبة فهو مفسدة بالمعنى العرفي وإن اشتمل على نفع مغلوب. ولذا كان الفعل ذو الوجهين منسوباً إلى الجهة الراجحة فيه من مصلحة أو مفسدة، أي من نفع أو ضرر.
وعلى هذا فكل شيء أو فعل إنما يكون مشروعاً أو ممنوعاً رجحان نفعه أو رجحان ضرره لا لأنه نفع محض أو ضرر محض. وتقدير ذلك إلى نظر الشارع.
ومن المقرر في هذه الحال أنَّ الجهة المرجوحة المغلوبة من نفع أو ضرر غير مقصودة للشارع في أوامره ونواهيه بل هي متغاضى عنها في سبيل الجهة الراجحة.
- ففي المطلوبات يكون الضرر المرجوح نظير الأشواك في الطريق الموصل إلى الغاية فهذه الأشواك غير مقصودة الوجود بالنسبة إلى السالك
- وفي المكروهات يكون النفع المرجوح نظير الربح المادي في عمل شائن يأباه الإنسان فذلك الربح غير مقصود الترك لذاته، بل مقصود الشخص في الترك إنما هو اجتناب العمل الشائن، لا اجتناب الربح.
ومن المقرر أيضاً أن المصالح التي يعتبرها الشرع ويرعاها لا عبرة لكونها موافقة لأهواء المكلفين وشهواتهم أو مخالفة، وإنما هي ما يقيم شأن الدنيا على أن تكون جسراً للآخرة، فتبني حياة صالحة فاضلة متعاونة على الخير والبر.
وقد أوضح الإمام الشاطبي هذا المعنى واستقصى الأدلة عليه، في «الموافقات» (٢٦/٢ و ٢٧) وقال:
وهذا النظر كله أساسه كون المصالح مشروعة لإقامة هذه الدنيا لا لنيل الشهوات ولا لإجابة داعي الهوى.

الباب الثاني : مصادر الفقه الإسلامي
٦/٥ - العوامل الداعية إلى الاستصلاح:
يمكننا بعد ما تقدم بيانه أن نقسم العوامل والدواعي التي توجب على الفقيه الشرعي أو الحاكم الأمر أن يلجأ إلى قاعدة الاستصلاح في استحداث أحكام جديدة في ظل الشريعة الإسلامية، إلى أربعة عوامل:
١ - جلب المصالح وهي الأمور التي يحتاج إليها المجتمع لإقامة حياة الناس على أقوم أساس كفرض الضرائب العادلة عند الحاجة لأجل تمويل الخدمات العامة والمشروعات الهامة المفيدة.
۲- درء المفاسد:
وهي الأمور التي تضر بالناس أفراداً أو جماعات سواء أكان ضررها مادياً أو خلقياً.
ومقياس الفساد هو قواعد الشريعة ومقاصدها المستفادة من نصوصها الثابتة، والتي يتألف منها نظام الإسلام.
٣ - سد الذرائع: أي منع الطرق التي تؤدي إلى إهمال أوامر الشريعة أو الاحتيال عليها أو تؤدي إلى الوقوع في محاذير شرعية ولو عن غير قصد.

فكل واحد من هذه العوامل الأربعة يدعو إلى سلوك طريق الاستصلاح باستحداث الأحكام المناسبة المحققة لغايات الشرع ومقاصده في إقامة الحياة الاجتماعية على أصلح منهاج ليكون منها في المجتمع أحسن نتاج. فأما العاملان الأولان وهما جلب المصالح ودرء المفاسد فمفهومهما وأمثلتهما واضحة مما سبق. وسنرى مزيداً من أمثلتهما قريباً عند كلامنا على أنواع الأحكام التي تبنى على نظرية المصالح المرسلة.
Chapter (5): Subsidiary Sources: Al-Istislah
A benefit in the customary sense, even if it includes a subjugated harm. And if the aspect of harm is predominant, then it is a harm in the customary sense, even if it includes a subjugated benefit. Hence, an act with two aspects is attributed to the predominant aspect, whether benefit or harm, i.e., profit or loss.
Based on this, every thing or act is either permissible or prohibited due to the preponderance of its benefit or the preponderance of its harm, not because it is pure benefit or pure harm. And assessing that is left to the consideration of the Lawgiver.
It is established in this case that the subjugated, overwhelmed aspect of benefit or harm is not intended by the Lawgiver in His commands and prohibitions; rather, it is overlooked for the sake of the predominant aspect.
- In desirable matters, the subjugated harm is like thorns on the road leading to the destination; these thorns are not intended for the traveler.
- In detestable matters, the subjugated benefit is like material profit in a shameful act that a person abhors; that profit is not intended to be abandoned for its own sake, but rather the person's intention in abandoning is only to avoid the shameful act, not to avoid the profit.
It is also established that the benefits considered and observed by the Law are not regarded based on whether they conform to the desires and passions of the obligated individuals or oppose them; rather, they are what establish the affairs of this world to be a bridge to the Hereafter, building a righteous, virtuous life cooperating in goodness and piety.
Imam al-Shatibi clarified this meaning and thoroughly examined the evidences for it in 'al-Muwafaqat' (2/26-27) and said:
All this consideration is based on the fact that benefits are legislated to establish this world, not to attain desires nor to respond to the call of whims.

Part Two: Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence
6/5 - Factors Prompting Istislah:
After what has been explained above, we can divide the factors and motives that oblige the jurist or ruler to resort to the principle of istislah in deriving new rulings within the framework of Islamic law into four factors:
1. Bringing benefits: they are the matters that the society needs to establish people's lives on the most solid foundation, such as imposing fair taxes when needed to finance public services and important beneficial projects.
2. Repelling harms: they are matters that harm people, individually or collectively, whether the harm is material or moral. The measure of corruption is the established rules and objectives of the Shari'ah derived from its definitive texts, which constitute the system of Islam.
3. Blocking the means (sadd al-dhara'i'): i.e., preventing the ways that lead to neglecting the commands of the Shari'ah or evading them, or that lead to falling into legal prohibitions even without intention.
4. Change of time: i.e., the difference in people's circumstances and general life conditions from what they were.
Each one of these four factors calls for adopting the path of istislah by deriving appropriate rulings that achieve the purposes and objectives of the Shari'ah in establishing social life on the most righteous path to produce the best results in society. As for the first two factors, i.e., bringing benefits and repelling harms, their concepts and examples are clear from what preceded. We will see more examples shortly when we discuss the types of rulings built on the theory of masalih mursalah.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy discussion on al-istislah (consideration of public interest) as a secondary source of Islamic law. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by defining maslaha (benefit) and mafsada (harm) in their customary sense, emphasizing that they are determined by the preponderance of benefit or harm, not by absolute purity. The Lawgiver (al-Shari') assesses this preponderance. He then explains that the subjugated aspect (either benefit in a harmful act or harm in a beneficial act) is overlooked for the sake of the predominant aspect, illustrating with examples: in desirable acts, subjugated harm is like thorns on a path; in detestable acts, subjugated benefit is like profit from a shameful act. He stresses that the benefits considered by the Shari'ah are not based on personal desires but on establishing worldly life as a bridge to the Hereafter, citing Imam al-Shatibi from al-Muwafaqat. The passage then introduces the second part: four factors that necessitate the use of istislah: (1) bringing benefits (jalb al-masalih), e.g., fair taxes for public services; (2) repelling harms (dar' al-mafasid), measured by the rules and objectives of the Shari'ah; (3) blocking the means (sadd al-dhara'i'), preventing paths to prohibition or evasion; and (4) change of time (taghayyur al-zaman), i.e., evolving circumstances. Each factor calls for deriving new rulings that achieve the Shari'ah's objectives. The first two factors' concepts and examples are clear from earlier discussion, and further examples will follow.

Key Terms
مصلحة (maslaha): Benefit in the customary sense, where the aspect of benefit is predominant over harm; it is the basis for istislah when conforming to Shar'i objectives.
مفسدة (mafsada): Harm in the customary sense, where the aspect of harm is predominant over benefit; it is to be repelled.
استصلاح (istislah): Deriving rulings based on considerations of public interest (maslaha mursalah) that are not explicitly indicated by a specific textual proof but are consistent with the general objectives of the Shari'ah.
سد الذرائع (sadd al-dhara'i'): Blocking the means or pathways that could lead to prohibited outcomes, even if the act itself is not prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the four factors that necessitate resorting to istislah?
A) Bringing benefits (jalb al-masalih)
B) Abrogation of previous rulings (naskh)
C) Blocking the means (sadd al-dhara'i')
D) Change of time (taghayyur al-zaman)

Key Takeaway
Istislah is a legitimate method for deriving rulings when new circumstances arise, provided it aligns with Shari'ah objectives and is based on the four factors of bringing benefit, repelling harm, blocking means, and adapting to change of time.

Sadd al-Dhara'i': The Principle of Blocking Means to Evil
Defining Sadd al-Dhara'i' as a source of Istislah with evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define Sadd al-Dhara'i', cite its Quranic evidence, and apply it to a novel scenario using Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Sadd al-Dhara'i' blocks any means—intentional or not—that leads to a prohibited outcome.

This lesson explores one of the four factors necessitating Istislah: blocking the means to evil (Sadd al-Dhara'i'). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that many prohibitions in Sharia are not because the acts themselves are inherently evil, but because they can lead to something prohibited. This principle is a branch of Istislah and is supported by Quranic evidence. By understanding this, you will see how Sharia prevents harm even before it occurs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic meaning
Define 'dhari'ah' as a means or path used to reach something. Recognize that in legal context, it often refers to a means leading to a prohibited end.
The term is not inherently negative; it is the outcome that determines the ruling.
2
Identify the legal principle
The principle of Sadd al-Dhara'i' states that if a permissible act is likely to lead to something prohibited, that act becomes prohibited. This includes both intentional and unintentional consequences.
Hanafis consider this under Istihsan, not as an independent source.
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence
The verse 6:108 prohibits insulting the idols of polytheists to prevent them from retaliating by insulting Allah. This is a clear example of blocking a means that would lead to a greater sin.
The prohibition of the means (insulting idols) is because of its likely consequence (insulting Allah).
4
Distinguish from other principles
Compare Sadd al-Dhara'i' with concepts like necessity (darurah) and public interest (maslahah mursalah). Note that Sadd al-Dhara'i' is preventive, while necessity is exceptional.
5
Apply the principle to a new case
Practice applying Sadd al-Dhara'i' to scenarios such as selling grapes to a winemaker or renting a building for a nightclub. Determine the ruling based on likelihood of leading to evil.
In Hanafi fiqh, such transactions are considered makruh tahrimi or fasid if the intention is clear.

إجمالية عنهما فيما يلي:
الأمور.
٧/٥ - سد الذرائع
(1)
الذريعة معناها في اللغة الوسيلة التي يلجأ إليها الإنسان لأمر من
كثيراً ما تكون الأعمال والتصرفات الممنوعة شرعاً ليست مقصودة لذاتها بالمنع في نظر الشارع، بل إنما منعت على خلاف مقتضى الأصل فيها لأنها قابلة أن تكون طرقاً مفضية إلى أمر ممنوع شرعاً ولو عن غير قصد، أو أن تكون ذريعة أي وسيلة يمكن أن يتشبث بها الإنسان عن قصد منه إلى ذلك الأمر الممنوع، وذلك من قبيل ما يسمى اليوم : الاحتيال
على القانون .
فلذا يمنع شرعاً كل طريق أو وسيلة قد تؤدي، عن قصد أو غير قصد إلى المحاذير الشرعية. ويسمى هذا الأصل في اصطلاح الفقهاء والأصوليين : مبدأ سد الذرائع، وهو باب واسع يتصل بسياسة التشريع،
ولذا يعتبر فرعاً
من الاستصلاح.
ويشهد لمبدأ سد الذرائع من نصوص الشريعة شواهد كثيرة جداً في
الكتاب والسنة .
فمن شواهده في الكتاب نهي القرآن العظيم عن سب أصنام المشركين
كيلا يثير ذلك حنقهم فيسبوا الله تعالى جهلاً وعدواناً، قال تعالى : لو ولا تسبوا الَّذِينَ يَدْعُونَ مِن دُونِ اللَّهِ فَيَسُبُوا اللَّهَ عَدوا بِغَيْرِ عِلْمٍ
(الأنعام: ١٠٨/٦).
(۱) صدرت أخيراً رسالة ضافية حول هذا الموضوع للأستاذ محمد هشام البرهاني (بيروت: مطبعة الريحاني، ١٤٠٦ هـ / ۱۹۸۵ م في ۸۸٠ صفحة).
Chapter Two: Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence
6/5 - Factors leading to Istislah:
After what has been explained, we can divide the factors and motives that obligate the Islamic jurist or ruler to resort to the principle of Istislah in developing new rulings within the framework of Islamic law into four factors:
1 - Bringing benefits: matters that society needs to establish people's lives on the most solid foundation, such as imposing fair taxes when needed to finance public services and important useful projects.
2 - Repelling harms: matters that harm people individually or collectively, whether the harm is material or moral. The measure of corruption is the rules of Sharia and its objectives derived from its established texts, which constitute the system of Islam.
3 - Blocking the means: preventing the paths that lead to neglecting the commands of Sharia or circumventing them, or lead to falling into legal prohibitions even if unintentionally.
4 - Change of time: the difference in people's conditions and general life circumstances from what they were.
Each of these four factors calls for following the path of Istislah by developing appropriate rulings that achieve the purposes and objectives of the law in establishing social life on the most sound method so that the best results come from it in society. As for the first two factors, bringing benefits and repelling harms, their concepts and examples are clear from what preceded. We will see more examples shortly when we discuss the types of rulings built on the theory of Mursalah interests.
Chapter (5) Subsidiary Sources: Istislah
As for the other two factors, blocking the means and change of time, we summarize an overall idea about them as follows:
7/5 - Blocking the Means
(1)
The meaning of al-dhari'ah in language is the means that a person resorts to for a matter. Often, actions and transactions prohibited by law are not intended for their own sake to be prohibited in the view of the Lawgiver, but rather they are prohibited contrary to the original ruling because they are likely to be paths leading to something prohibited by law even if unintentionally, or to be a means that a person can intentionally cling to that prohibited matter, and this is like what is called today: circumventing the law.
Therefore, every path or means that may lead, intentionally or unintentionally, to legal prohibitions is prohibited by law. This principle in the terminology of jurists and usul scholars is called: the principle of blocking the means (Sadd al-Dhara'i'), and it is a broad chapter connected to legislative policy, and thus it is considered a branch of Istislah.
The principle of blocking the means is witnessed by many evidences from the texts of the Sharia in the Quran and Sunnah.
Among its evidences in the Quran is the prohibition in the Great Quran of insulting the idols of polytheists, lest that provoke their anger so they insult Allah out of ignorance and aggression. Allah says: "And do not insult those they invoke besides Allah, lest they insult Allah in enmity without knowledge." (Al-An'am 6:108).
(1) A comprehensive thesis has recently been published on this topic by Professor Muhammad Hisham al-Burhani (Beirut: Al-Rayhani Press, 1406 AH / 1985 CE, 880 pages).
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa explains the factors that necessitate resorting to the principle of Istislah (public interest) in Islamic jurisprudence. The author lists four factors: bringing benefits, repelling harms, blocking means to evil, and change of time. The focus here is on the third factor: Sadd al-Dhara'i' (blocking the means). The author defines 'al-dhari'ah' linguistically as a means and then explains that many prohibitions in Sharia are not because the act itself is inherently evil, but because it can lead to something prohibited. This is the core of Sadd al-Dhara'i': preventing any path that could lead to a prohibited outcome, whether intentionally or unintentionally. He emphasizes that this principle is a broad field connected to legislative policy and is considered a branch of Istislah. The Quranic verse prohibiting the insult of idols (6:108) is cited as evidence, because it blocks the means that would lead to the retaliation of polytheists insulting Allah. This is a classic example of Sadd al-Dhara'i'. The passage underscores the importance of this principle in preventing harm and preserving the objectives of Sharia.

Key Terms
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): A method of deriving rulings based on public interest not explicitly covered by primary sources
الذريعة (al-dhari'ah): The means or path that leads to a certain matter, often used in legal context as a means to an evil end
سد الذرائع (sadd al-dhara'i'): The principle of blocking means that may lead to forbidden acts

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the principle of 'سد الذرائع' (Sadd al-Dhara'i')?
A) It is the principle of preferring public interest over private.
B) It is the principle of blocking means that lead to forbidden acts.
C) It is the principle of necessity overriding prohibitions.
D) It is the principle of considering custom as a source of law.

Key Takeaway
Sadd al-Dhara'i' is a preventive principle in Islamic law that prohibits permissible acts when they are likely to lead to a prohibited outcome, even if unintentionally.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
12 min
Mabda' Sad al-Dhara'i' (Blocking the Means) as a Subsidiary Source
Definition, proofs, and analysis of Sad al-Dhara'i' as a subsidiary source of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the principle of Sad al-Dhara'i', explain its basis in the Quran and Sunnah, and apply it to identify which means are blocked in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Block the pathway to harm: permissible acts become prohibited if they typically lead to sin, even without evil intent.

In Hanafi usul, Sad al-Dhara'i' is a subsidiary source that prohibits acts that are themselves permissible but predictably lead to forbidden outcomes. This principle is rooted in numerous Quranic verses and Prophetic hadith. Understanding it allows the mufti to preemptively block loopholes and protect the objectives of Shari'ah. In this lesson, we analyze its definition, evidence, and application.

Guided Path
1
Define al-Dhari'ah linguistically and technically
Start with the linguistic meaning: al-dhari'ah is the means by which one reaches an end. Technically, it refers to acts that, though permissible in origin, are likely to lead to a prohibited result.
The emphasis is on likelihood, not mere possibility.
2
Identify the rationale behind blocking means
The Lawgiver prohibits not only the harmful outcome but also the path that consistently leads to it. This is a preventative measure to maintain the integrity of Shari'ah objectives.
Contrast with the principle that acts are judged by intentions; here, the objective consequence matters.
3
Analyze the Quranic evidences
Examine the two examples: reviling idols (lest it lead to reviling Allah) and proposing during 'iddah (lest it lead to violating the waiting period). Identify the prohibited outcome and the blocked means.
The block applies even when the means itself is not sinful.
4
Analyze the Prophetic evidences
Study the hadith: building mosques over graves, wills for heirs, and seclusion with non-mahram. For each, link the means to the potential harm.
The prohibition of wills for heirs prevents circumvention of fixed inheritance shares.
5
Apply the principle to new cases
Practice identifying means that should be blocked. Consider scenarios like selling grapes to a winemaker or renting property for a riba-based bank.
The Hanafi school uses this principle extensively in muamalat.

الفصل (5) المصادر التبعية: الاستصلاح
وأما العاملان الآخران، وهما سد الذرائع وتغير الزمان، فنوجز فكرة إجمالية عنهما فيما يلي:

7/5 - سد الذرائع
الذريعة معناها في اللغة الوسيلة التي يلجأ إليها الإنسان لأمر من الأمور.
كثيراً ما تكون الأعمال والتصرفات الممنوعة شرعاً ليست مقصودة لذاتها بالمنع في نظر الشارع، بل إنما منعت على خلاف مقتضى الأصل فيها لأنها قابلة أن تكون طرقاً مفضية إلى أمر ممنوع شرعاً ولو عن غير قصد، أو أن تكون ذريعة أي وسيلة يمكن أن يتشبث بها الإنسان عن قصد منه إلى ذلك الأمر الممنوع، وذلك من قبيل ما يسمى اليوم: الاحتيال على القانون.
فلذا يمنع شرعاً كل طريق أو وسيلة قد تؤدي، عن قصد أو غير قصد إلى المحاذير الشرعية. ويسمى هذا الأصل في اصطلاح الفقهاء والأصوليين: مبدأ سد الذرائع، وهو باب واسع يتصل بسياسة التشريع، ولذا يعتبر فرعاً من الاستصلاح.
ويشهد لمبدأ سد الذرائع من نصوص الشريعة شواهد كثيرة جداً في الكتاب والسنة.
فمن شواهده في الكتاب نهي القرآن العظيم عن سب أصنام المشركين كيلا يثير ذلك حنقهم فيسبوا الله تعالى جهلاً وعدواناً، قال تعالى: {ولا تسبوا الذين يدعون من دون الله فيسبوا الله عدوا بغير علم} (الأنعام: 108/6). (1)
وكذلك حرم القرآن خطبة المرأة المعتدة بعد انقضاء زواجها بطلاق أو بوفاة الزوج حتى تنقضي عدتها كي لا تؤدي خطبتها إلى إخلال بواجب العدة الخطير الذي يقتضيه نظام الأسرة وحقوق الزوجية السابقة. ولذا كان أيضاً عقد الزواج على المرأة المعتدة، ولو بلا دخول غير منعقد شرعاً.
ومن شواهد مبدأ سد الذرائع في السنة النبوية أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام نهى عن بناء المساجد على القبور وذلك كيلا تفضي إلى عبادة الموتى من عظماء الناس.
وكذلك منع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الوصية للوارث كي لا تتخذ ذريعة إلى تفضيل بعض الورثة على بعض احتيالاً على نظام الإرث، فإن هذا التفضيل ممنوع شرعاً كما سنرى في (8/23)(1).
وكذلك نهى النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام عن خلوة الرجل بامرأة أجنبية عنه، لأن هذه الخلوة ذريعة يمكن أن تفضي إلى الفساد، ومن الواجب درء المفاسد.
يتضح من هذه الأمثلة الملاحظات التالية:
1 - أن مبدأ سد الذرائع سلكته الشريعة الإسلامية في الأمور الدينية والمدنية على السواء. فالدينية كمنع بناء المساجد على القبور، والمدنية كالوصية للوارث.
2 - أن المنظور إليه في سد الذرائع ليس هو النية السيئة من الفاعل، بل مجرد كون الفعل مما يفضي إلى النتيجة التي يأباها الشرع، ولو كان (1) هذا رأي الجمهور. وذهب الزيدية والإمامية إلى القول بنفاذ الوصية للوارث كغير الوارث في حدود ثلث التركة بحجة أن قول الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم بعد نزول آيات المواريث: ألا لا وصية لوارث هو لنسخ وجوب الوصية للقريب الذي أصبح وارثاً، إذ كان الوجوب هو المقرر بنص القرآن قبل تشريع الإرث فيبقى الحكم على الجواز بعد نسخ الوجوب.
Chapter (5): Subsidiary Sources: al-Istislah (Consideration of Public Interest). As for the other two factors, namely Sad al-Dhara'i' (Blocking the Means) and Taghayyur al-Zaman (Change of Time), we summarize an overall idea about them as follows:

7/5 - Sad al-Dhara'i'
The linguistic meaning of al-Dhari'ah is the means to which a person resorts for a matter among matters. Often, actions and transactions that are prohibited by Shari'ah are not intended for prohibition in themselves in the view of the Lawgiver; rather, they are prohibited contrary to the original ruling because they are liable to be paths leading to a matter prohibited by Shari'ah, even unintentionally, or they may be a means by which a person may deliberately cling to that prohibited matter. This is akin to what is today called 'circumvention of the law.'
Hence, Shari'ah prohibits every path or means that may lead, intentionally or unintentionally, to prohibited matters. This principle is termed by jurists and usul scholars as the principle of Sad al-Dhara'i' (Blocking the Means). It is a broad topic connected to legislative policy, and therefore it is considered a branch of al-Istislah (Consideration of Public Interest).
Numerous evidences from the texts of Shari'ah, in the Quran and Sunnah, bear witness to the principle of Sad al-Dhara'i'.
Among its evidences from the Quran is the prohibition by the Glorious Quran of reviling the idols of the polytheists, lest that provoke their anger and they revile Allah out of ignorance and transgression. Allah, Most High, said: {And do not revile those whom they invoke besides Allah, lest they revile Allah in enmity without knowledge} (Al-An'am 6:108). (1)
Similarly, the Quran prohibited proposing marriage to a woman in her 'iddah (waiting period) after the dissolution of her marriage by divorce or death of the husband until her 'iddah expires, lest the proposal lead to a breach of the serious duty of 'iddah required by the family system and previous marital rights. Hence, the marriage contract with a woman in her 'iddah, even without consummation, is not validly contracted according to Shari'ah.
Among the evidences of the principle of Sad al-Dhara'i' from the Prophetic Sunnah is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade building mosques over graves, lest it lead to the worship of the dead among great people.
Likewise, the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited a will in favor of an heir, lest it be taken as a means to prefer some heirs over others in circumvention of the inheritance system, for such preference is prohibited by Shari'ah as will be seen in (8/23)(1).
Similarly, the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited a man's seclusion with an unrelated woman, because such seclusion is a means that may lead to corruption, and it is obligatory to repel evils.
From these examples, the following observations become clear:
1 - That the principle of Sad al-Dhara'i' has been adopted by Islamic Shari'ah in both religious and civil matters alike. Religious matters include the prohibition of building mosques over graves, and civil matters include the will in favor of an heir.
2 - That what is considered in Sad al-Dhara'i' is not the evil intention of the doer, but rather the mere fact that the act leads to a result that Shari'ah disapproves, even if (1) This is the view of the majority. The Zaydis and Imamis hold that a will in favor of an heir is valid like any other heir up to one-third of the estate, arguing that the Prophet's statement after the revelation of the inheritance verses: 'No will for an heir' is abrogation of the obligation of a will for the relative who became an heir, since obligation was established by the Quran before the legislation of inheritance, so the ruling remains permissibility after the abrogation of obligation.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage introduces Sad al-Dhara'i' (Blocking the Means) as a subsidiary source of Islamic law. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, defines al-dhari'ah as a means or pathway by which one may reach an objective. The core idea is that even permissible acts can be prohibited if they typically lead to a prohibited outcome, regardless of the actor's intention. This is not about punishing evil intentions alone; it is about preemptively blocking pathways to harm. The author provides Quranic and Prophetic evidence: the Quran forbids insulting idols (lest it lead to insulting Allah), and prohibits proposing to a woman in 'iddah (lest it lead to violating the waiting period). From the Sunnah, he cites the prohibition of building mosques over graves (to avoid worship of the dead), making a will for an heir (to avoid circumventing inheritance rules), and seclusion with a non-mahram woman (to prevent immorality). The author then draws two observations: (1) this principle applies equally in religious and civil spheres, and (2) the criterion is the objective likelihood of leading to harm, not the doer's intention. A footnote clarifies a minority view from the Zaydi and Imami schools regarding wills for heirs, which is rejected by the majority (including Hanafis). The Hanafi school strongly adheres to Sad al-Dhara'i', as seen in rulings such as the prohibition of selling grapes to a winemaker or lending money with interest-based transactions.

Key Terms
سد الذرائع (sadd al-dharā’i‘): Blocking the means that lead to prohibited results, even if the means themselves are originally permissible.
الاستصلاح (al-istiṣlāḥ): Consideration of public interest as a source of law; Sad al-Dhara'i' is a branch of this.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best exemplifies the principle of Sad al-Dhara'i' (Blocking the Means)?
A) Prohibiting wine because it is intoxicating.
B) Prohibiting the sale of grapes to a known winemaker because it leads to the production of wine.
C) Permitting the sale of grapes because grapes themselves are permissible.
D) Punishing a person for intending to commit theft although they did not act.

Key Takeaway
The principle of Sad al-Dhara'i' is a powerful tool in Hanafi fiqh to preemptively block means to harm, applied regardless of the actor's intent, and is supported by numerous texts from the Quran and Sunnah.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

How Later Hanafi Jurists Applied Istislah: Coercion and Waqf Leases
Examples of fatwa change due to time using istislah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how later Hanafi jurists used istislah (consideration of public interest) to modify rulings in response to changing social conditions.

Core Idea
When circumstances change, the ruling may change – istislah allows Hanafi jurists to serve public interest without contradicting the school's principles.

This passage illustrates the dynamic nature of Hanafi fiqh. While the school has fixed principles, later jurists can depart from earlier explicit rulings if they determine that the earlier imam would have ruled differently given the new context. The key mechanism is istislah, i.e., considering the public interest and the objectives of Sharia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original ruling
What did the earlier Hanafi jurists say about coercion (ikrah) and waqf leasing?
For coercion: it was only an excuse if from the ruler. For waqf: long-term leases were permitted.
2
Identify the changed circumstance
What social problem arose that made the original ruling impractical?
Corruption and lack of deterrence for coercion; usurpation of waqf properties by influential people.
3
Identify the new ruling
What did the later jurists adopt?
Coercion is an excuse even from non-rulers; waqf leases restricted to one year (buildings) or three years (land).
4
Identify the underlying rationale
How does this serve istislah (public interest)?
Prevents hardship and protects property rights; the general Sharia principle of removing hardship and repelling harm.
5
Connect to the principle
Why is this not a contradiction but an application of the school's own principles?
The later jurists believed the earlier imam would have ruled the same way if he had witnessed the changed conditions.

الفصل (٥) المصادر التبعية الاستصلاح
ولهذا ترى فقهاء المذهب خالفوا ما نصَّ عليه المجتهد في مواضع كثيرة بناها على ما كان في زمنه لعلمهم بأنه لو كان في زمنهم لقال بما قالوا به أخذاً من قواعد مذهبه .
وقد أتى رحمه الله على ذلك بأمثلة كثيرة :
- منها أنه في أصل المذهب الحنفي لم يكن يعتبر الإكراه معذرة للشخص المستكره (بفتح الراء) إلا إذا كان واقعاً من السلطان نفسه أو بأمره، لأنَّ قيام سلطة الحاكم تمنع الناس من أن يُكره بعضهم بعضاً على فعل أو عقد، إذ يستطيع من يقع عليه الإكراه أن يلتجيء إلى الحاكم. أما إذا وقع الإكراه من السلطان نفسه فلا سلطة فوقه، وبيده القوة.
لكن المتأخرين من الفقهاء لما رأوا فساد الزمان وتسلط بعض الناس على بعض تحت حماية بعض الحكام ولحظوا فقدان الوازع أفتوا بأنَّ الإكراه يعتبر معذرة مؤثرة في مسؤولية المستكره عن الأفعال والعقود التي باشرها تحت الإكراه ولو كان واقعاً من غير السلطان الحاكم.
-
ومنها أنَّ الفقهاء المتقدمين أيضاً كانوا يجيزون إيجار عقارات الوقف مهما كانت مدة الإيجار طويلة أو قصيرة.
ولكن المتأخرين لما رأوا كثرة غصب المتنفذين لأملاك الأوقاف وتواطؤ بعض المتولين على الأوقاف معهم أفتوا بمنع إيجار عقار الوقف أكثر من سنة واحدة في الدور والحوانيت المبنية، وثلاث سنين في الأراضي الزراعية، خشية أن يدعي المستأجر في النهاية ملكية العقار، متخذاً استمرار يده وتصرفه فيه زمناً طويلاً دليلاً على ملكيته. فتجديد عقد الإيجار في فترات قصيرة يحول دون ذلك (ر) مجموعة رسائل ابن عابدين ١٢٥/٢ - ١٢٦).
الباب الثاني: مصادر الفقه الإسلامي
- ۷۸ لما بين العرف وتغير الزمان من صلة، فنجتزيء هنا بهذه اللمحة الإجمالية .
٩/٥ - الوفق والفرق بين الاستصلاح والاستحسان :
إن الاستحسان عند الحنفية كما رأينا نوعان متباينان :
فأحدهما وهو الاستحسان القياسي - إنما هو ضرب من القياس، إذ هو ترجيح قياس خفي الملحظ قوي المبنى على قياس ظاهر متبادر ، عند تعدد وجوه القياس وتعارضها في مسألة ما.
:
والثاني ـ وهو استحسان الضرورة ـ هو في الحقيقة ضرب من الاستصلاح أي فرع من قاعدة المصالح المرسلة، لأنَّ فيه عدولاً عن مقتضى حكم القياس في قضية إلى حكم آخر إذا كان ذلك القياس يؤدي إلى حرج غير عادي أو إلى ضرر غالب أي ضرر أعظم من المصلحة المتوخاة، ذلك لأن منهاج الشريعة العام المقرر في نصوصها الأصلية يقوم على أساس نفي الحرج، ودفع الضرر الغالب من طريق أحكامها
أما عند المالكية فإن الاستحسان هو دائماً فرع من نظرية المصالح لأن الاستحسان عندهم نوع واحد هو العدول عن القياس رعاية لمصلحة تعارضه في مسألة معينة.
فهم لا يسمون القياس الخفي استحساناً وإنما هو «قياس» باق على أصل اسمه.
الاستحسان وقاعدة
ما كان
غير أن هناك نقطة واحدة تعد هي الفارق بين المصالح المرسلة وهي أن الحكم الاستحساني في مسألة هو مخالفاً لمقتضى القواعد القياسية فيها على سبيل الاستثناء من تلك القواعد، إما رعاية لمصلحة عامة كتضمين الأجراء العموميين ما في أيديهم من أموال الناس ( ر : ف (٦/٤) ، وكتعجيل انحلال الزواج بين المرأة وزوجها المفقود
Chapter (5): The Subsidiary Sources - Istislah. For this reason you see the jurists of the school contradict what the mujtahid stipulated in many places based on what was in his time, knowing that if he were in their time he would have said what they said, taking from the principles of his school. And he (the author) gave many examples of that: - Among them is that in the original Hanafi school, coercion was not considered an excuse for the coerced person unless it came from the ruler himself or by his order, because the existence of the ruler's authority prevents people from coercing each other into an action or contract, since the one coerced can resort to the ruler. But if coercion comes from the ruler himself, there is no authority above him and he has power. However, the later jurists, when they saw the corruption of the time and the domination of some people over others under the protection of some rulers, and they noticed the lack of deterrence, they issued fatwas that coercion is considered an effective excuse in the responsibility of the coerced for the acts and contracts he performed under coercion, even if it is from someone other than the ruling sultan. - Another example: The earlier jurists also permitted leasing waqf real estate regardless of the lease period, long or short. But the later jurists, when they saw the frequent usurpation of waqf properties by the influential and the collusion of some trustees with them, issued fatwas prohibiting the leasing of waqf real estate for more than one year for houses and built shops, and three years for agricultural lands, fearing that the lessee would eventually claim ownership of the property, taking his continuous possession and disposition over a long time as evidence of ownership. Renewing the lease contract in short periods prevents that. (See: Ibn Abidin's Collection of Treatises 2/125-126). Part Two: Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence - 78: Because of the connection between custom and the change of time, we suffice here with this brief overview. 9/5 - Harmony and Difference between Istislah and Istihsan: Indeed, istihsan according to the Hanafis, as we have seen, is of two distinct types: One of them - the qiyasic istihsan - is a type of qiyas, as it is a preference for a hidden analogy with a strong basis over an apparent immediate analogy, when there are multiple aspects of analogy conflict in a particular issue. The second - istihsan of necessity - is in reality a type of istislah, i.e., a branch of the principle of masalih mursala, because it involves deviating from the requirement of the qiyas ruling in a case to another ruling if that qiyas leads to unusual hardship or to a prevailing harm, i.e., a harm greater than the intended benefit. That is because the general methodology of Sharia established in its original texts is based on removing hardship and repelling prevailing harm through its rulings. As for the Malikis, istihsan is always a branch of the theory of masalih, because istihsan for them is one type: deviating from qiyas in favor of a maslaha that conflicts with it in a particular issue. They do not call hidden analogy istihsan; rather it remains 'qiyas' under its original name. Istihsan and the principle of 'what was' but there is one point that is the difference between it and masalih mursala: that the istihsani ruling in a matter contradicts what the qiyas principles require in it by way of exception from those principles, either in consideration of a public interest, such as holding public employees liable for what is in their hands of people's money (see: F: 6/4), and such as accelerating the dissolution of marriage between a woman and her missing husband.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage discusses the subsidiary source of istislah (consideration of public interest) in Hanafi jurisprudence. It begins by observing that later Hanafi jurists sometimes departed from the explicit rulings of earlier imams when circumstances changed, rightly believing that the earlier imam would have ruled differently had he lived in their time. Two concrete examples are provided. First, the early Hanafi position held that coercion (ikrah) was only an excuse if it came from the ruler, because the ruler's authority could deter private coercion. But later jurists, seeing societal decay and the inability of victims to seek justice, ruled that coercion is an excuse even from private parties. Second, early jurists permitted long-term leases of waqf property; later jurists, witnessing widespread usurpation, restricted leases to one year for buildings and three for agricultural land. The passage then transitions to a comparison between istihsan and istislah. For Hanafis, istihsan is of two types: qiyasic istihsan (preferring a hidden analogy) and necessity istihsan (which is actually a form of istislah, i.e., maslaha mursala). The latter is invoked when strict qiyas leads to undue hardship or harm. For Malikis, all istihsan is a branch of maslaha; they do not consider hidden analogy as istihsan. The passage concludes that the difference between istihsan and maslaha mursala is that an istihsani ruling is an exception to qiyas principles for a particular public interest, like holding public employees liable or dissolving marriage for a missing husband. The discussion is cut off at this point.

Key Terms
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): Consideration of public interest; deviating from strict analogy to avoid hardship or harm, based on the general objectives of Sharia.
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Coercion; compelling someone to do something against their will under threat.
الوقف (al-waqf): Endowment; property dedicated for charitable purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did later Hanafi jurists change the ruling on coercion (ikrah) to consider it an excuse even when coming from a non-ruler?
A) Because a new Quranic verse was revealed.
B) Because they observed societal corruption and loss of deterrence, making it difficult for the coerced to seek justice.
C) Because of a consensus among later scholars.
D) Because the earlier ruling was based on a weak hadith.

Key Takeaway
Istislah allows Hanafi jurists to adjust rulings when circumstances change, as long as the new ruling is consistent with the school's principles and serves the public interest.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Hanafi-Maliki Dispute on Istihsan and Its Relation to Istislah
Comparing Hanafi and Maliki views on istihsan and its relation to istislah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi and Maliki definitions of istihsan and explain how Hanafi necessity istihsan is a branch of istislah.

Core Idea
Hanafis split istihsan into qiyasic (hidden analogy) and necessity (a form of istislah); Malikis treat all istihsan as departure from qiyas for maslaha.

This passage clarifies a common confusion: istihsan is not a monolithic concept. The Hanafi school has a nuanced view, distinguishing between a type that is still qiyas (hidden analogy) and a type that is actually istislah (public interest). The Maliki school, on the other hand, equates all istihsan with maslaha. Understanding this difference is crucial for proper fatwa derivation, as it affects how jurists justify rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi two-part definition
What are the two types of istihsan according to the Hanafis?
Type 1: qiyasic istihsan (preferring hidden analogy). Type 2: necessity istihsan (departure from qiyas due to hardship).
2
Define each type
Explain qiyasic and necessity istihsan.
Qiyasic: hidden analogy is still qiyas. Necessity: actually a branch of istislah (maslaha mursala).
3
Contrast with the Maliki definition
How do Malikis define istihsan?
One type only: departure from qiyas for a conflicting maslaha. They do not call hidden analogy istihsan.
4
Identify the point of agreement
What do both schools accept?
Both accept departure from qiyas for maslaha in certain cases (the Hanafi necessity type is similar to Maliki istihsan).
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence of this difference?
Hanafi fatwa can use hidden analogy without invoking maslaha; Maliki always needs a maslaha rationale. Also, the scope of istihsan is wider for Hanafis.

وقد أتى رحمه الله على ذلك بأمثلة كثيرة :

Commentary
This passage discusses the subsidiary source of istislah (consideration of public interest) in Hanafi jurisprudence.
