Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Preferring a hidden analogy over a manifest one (qiyasic) or departing from qiyas due to necessity (necessity istihsan).
القياس الخفي (al-qiyas al-khafi): A subtle analogy that is less apparent but has a stronger basis than the manifest analogy.
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): Consideration of public interest; deviating from strict analogy to avoid hardship or harm.
المصالح المرسلة (al-masalih al-mursala): Unregulated public interests not explicitly addressed by textual evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, how does the Maliki view on istihsan differ from the Hanafi view?
A) Malikis consider hidden analogy as istihsan, Hanafis do not.
B) Malikis have two types of istihsan, Hanafis have one.
C) Malikis consider all istihsan as departure from qiyas for maslaha, while Hanafis distinguish a type that is still qiyas (hidden analogy).
D) Malikis reject istihsan altogether, Hanafis accept it.

Key Takeaway
Hanafis categorize istihsan into qiyasic (still qiyas) and necessity (istislah), while Malikis treat all istihsan as a departure from qiyas for maslaha, excluding hidden analogy.

The Relationship between Istihsan and Istislah in Hanafi and Maliki Fiqh
Comparative legal philosophy of istihsan and istislah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of istihsan in the Hanafi school and explain how istihsan relates to maslaha mursala (istislah).

Core Idea
Hanafi istihsan includes hidden qiyas and necessity-based departure; the latter is istislah.

In this lesson, we explore the nuanced relationship between istihsan and istislah as articulated by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa. The Hanafi school recognizes two distinct types of istihsan: one that is a form of qiyas (preferring a hidden analogy) and another that is a departure from qiyas due to necessity or overriding harm. The latter, as we shall see, is essentially a branch of istislah (maslaha mursala). The Maliki school, by contrast, treats istihsan solely as a departure from qiyas for the sake of a maslaha, rejecting the classification of hidden qiyas as istihsan. This comparative analysis will sharpen your understanding of how these two schools operationalize public interest in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define the two types of istihsan in the Hanafi school
Identify the key characteristics of qiyasi istihsan (preference for a hidden qiyas over an apparent one) and daruri istihsan (departure from qiyas due to hardship or predominant harm). Note that the Hanafis consider the first as a type of qiyas, not a true departure.
Daruri istihsan is described as a 'branch of the principle of masalih mursala'.
2
Compare with the Maliki definition of istihsan
In the Maliki school, istihsan is always a departure from qiyas in favor of a maslaha. They do not consider hidden qiyas as istihsan; it remains qiyas. This is a key point of divergence.
The text states: 'They do not call hidden qiyas istihsan but rather it remains qiyas by its original name.'
3
Identify the key difference between istihsan and istislah
Istihsan involves an exception to an existing qiyas rule, whereas istislah (maslaha mursala) can operate in areas where no qiyas exists. Istislah is thus broader and does not necessarily conflict with qiyas.
Examples: holding public employees liable (istihsan) vs. imposing taxes (istislah).
4
Apply the concepts to a concrete example from the text
Work through the example of khiyar al-shart inheritance from the footnote. In the Maliki view, istihsan permits upholding the contract despite the heirs' disagreement, while in the Hanafi view, khiyar al-shart is not inherited, so no istihsan is needed.
This illustrates the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between the schools' approaches.
5
Synthesize the relationship using al-Shatibi's observation
Al-Shatibi notes that Maliki istihsan is often maslaha mursala, but they frame it as an exception to rules. This aligns with the Hanafi depiction of daruri istihsan as a subset of istislah.
The upshot: necessity-based istihsan = istislah; qiyasi istihsan = a type of qiyas.

٩/٥ - الوفق والفرق بين الاستصلاح والاستحسان :
إن الاستحسان عند الحنفية كما رأينا نوعان متباينان :
فأحدهما - وهو الاستحسان القياسي - إنما هو ضرب من القياس، إذ هو ترجيح قياس خفي الملحظ قوي المبنى على قياس ظاهر متبادر ، عند تعدد وجوه القياس وتعارضها في مسألة ما.
والثاني - وهو استحسان الضرورة - هو في الحقيقة ضرب من الاستصلاح أي فرع من قاعدة المصالح المرسلة، لأنَّ فيه عدولاً عن مقتضى حكم القياس في قضية إلى حكم آخر إذا كان ذلك القياس يؤدي إلى حرج غير عادي أو إلى ضرر غالب أي ضرر أعظم من المصلحة المتوخاة، ذلك لأن منهاج الشريعة العام المقرر في نصوصها الأصلية يقوم على أساس نفي الحرج، ودفع الضرر الغالب من طريق أحكامها.
أما عند المالكية فإن الاستحسان هو دائماً فرع من نظرية المصالح لأن الاستحسان عندهم نوع واحد هو العدول عن القياس رعاية لمصلحة تعارضه في مسألة معينة.
فهم لا يسمون القياس الخفي استحساناً وإنما هو «قياس» باق على أصل اسمه.
الاستحسان وقاعدة المصالح المرسلة:
غير أن هناك نقطة واحدة تعد هي الفارق بين المصالح المرسلة وهي أن الحكم الاستحساني في مسألة هو مخالفاً لمقتضى القواعد القياسية فيها على سبيل الاستثناء من تلك القواعد، إما رعاية لمصلحة عامة كتضمين الأجراء العموميين ما في أيديهم من أموال الناس (ر : ف (٦/٤) ، وكتعجيل انحلال الزواج بين المرأة وزوجها المفقود (ر ف (۱۲/؛ وإما رعاية لمصلحة حقوقية جزئية (١).
أما المصلحة المرسلة التي يبنى عليها الاستصلاح فلا يشترط أن يكون فيها مخالفة لقياس يعارضها. فقد يكون الحكم الثابت بها في الشؤون العامة التي لا يوجد في الشريعة دليل على خلافها بل المصلحة فيها هي الدليل الوحيد كفرض الضرائب الإلزامية عند الحاجة وتحديد عقوبات الجرائم منعاً لفوضى الأحكام باختلاف أنظار الحكام وكإلزام الحكومة الناس بالقضاء بمذهب فقهي مخصوص، أو تقنين أحكام مدنية مختارة من عدة مذاهب فقهية اجتهادية معتبرة، لكي يعلم الناس مصير معاملاتهم وعقودهم، ويعرفوا وجهة القضاء فيها سلفاً بصورة ثابتة مستقرة.
فكل ذلك وأمثاله مما تتجدد الحاجات فيه ولا تحصى، ولا يوجد في الشريعة دلائل قياسية كانت تقتضي خلافه، إنما هو استصلاح محض . يقول الإمام أبو إسحاق الشاطبي في كتابه «الاعتصام» بعد ذكره مسائل من صور الاستحسان عند الإمام مالك:
فإن قيل : هذا من باب المصالح المرسلة لا من باب الاستحسان؟ قلنا: نعم، إلا أنهم صوَّروا الاستحسان تصوير الاستثناء من القواعد؛

(١) من الأمثلة التي يذكرها المالكية للاستحسان المبني على رعاية مصلحة جزئية ما لو أحد شيئاً على أنه بالخيار في إبرام العقد وفسخه خلال مدة معينة - وهو المسمى خيار الشرط - ثم مات المشتري قبل انقضاء المدة المضروبة، فاختلف ورثته : فرضي بعضهم بإبرام العقد ورفض بعضهم، ولم يقبل البائع تجزئة المبيع. فمقتضى القياس أن يبطل البيع في الكل. ولكنهم استحسنوا إمضاء العقد على البائع إذا قبل من رضي من الورثة بأن يأخذ حصة من رفض وذلك رعاية لمصلحة الوارث دون أن يتضرر البائع لانتفاء التجزئة.
وهذا الاستحسان في هذه المسألة لا يجري في الاجتهاد الحنفي لأن من المقرر فيه أن خيار الشرط لا يورث فيموت صاحب الخيار خلال المدة المضروبة ينبرم ويستقر ملك المشتري في المبيع نهائياً إن كان الخيار للبائع، أو يثبت الملك فيه لورثة المشتري إن كان الخيار له فمات (ر : المجلة / ٣٠٦).
9/5 - The Agreement and Difference between Istislah and Istihsan:
Indeed, istihsan according to the Hanafis, as we have seen, is of two distinct types:
The first - which is qiyasi istihsan - is a type of qiyas, because it is the preference of a hidden-analogy, strong-based qiyas over an apparent, immediate qiyas when there are multiple aspects of qiyas and they conflict in a certain issue.
The second - which is istihsan al-darura (necessity-based istihsan) - is in reality a type of istislah, i.e., a branch of the principle of masalih mursala (unrestricted interests), because it involves departing from the requirement of the ruling of qiyas in a case to another ruling if that qiyas leads to unusual hardship or a prevailing harm, i.e., a harm greater than the intended benefit. This is because the general methodology of the Shari'a, established in its original texts, is based on the principle of negating hardship and repelling prevailing harm through its rulings.
As for the Malikis, istihsan is always a branch of the theory of interests (masalih), because istihsan according to them is one type: departing from qiyas in consideration of an interest that conflicts with it in a particular issue.
Thus they do not call hidden qiyas istihsan, but rather it remains 'qiyas' by its original name.
Istihsan and the Principle of Masalih Mursala:
However, there is one point that is the difference between them and masalih mursala: the ruling based on istihsan in a matter is contrary to what is required by the qiyas rules in it by way of an exception from those rules, either in consideration of a public interest, such as holding public employees liable for what is in their hands of people's wealth (see: F, 6/4), and such as hastening the dissolution of marriage between a woman and her missing husband (see: F, 12/); or in consideration of a partial contractual interest (1).
As for the maslaha mursala on which istislah is based, it is not necessary that it involve conflict with a qiyas that opposes it. The ruling established by it may be in public affairs for which there is no evidence in the Shari'a to the contrary; rather, the interest is the only evidence, such as imposing compulsory taxes when needed, prescribing penalties for crimes to prevent chaos of rulings due to differing views of judges, and such as the government obliging people to adjudicate according to a particular juristic school, or codifying civil rulings selected from several respected ijtihadi juristic schools, so that people may know the outcome of their transactions and contracts, and know the direction of adjudication in them beforehand in a stable and settled manner.
All that and the like, for which needs are constantly renewing and cannot be counted, and there are no qiyas evidence in the Shari'a that would require the opposite, is pure istislah. Imam Abu Ishaq al-Shatibi said in his book 'al-I'tisam' after mentioning issues from examples of istihsan according to Imam Malik:
If it is said: This is from the chapter of masalih mursala, not from the chapter of istihsan? We say: Yes, except that they portrayed istihsan as a portrayal of an exception from the rules.

(1) Among the examples mentioned by the Malikis for istihsan based on consideration of a partial interest is: If someone buys something on the condition that he has the option to conclude the contract or rescind it within a specified period - which is called khiyar al-shart (option of condition) - then the buyer dies before the specified period expires, and his heirs disagree: some agree to conclude the contract and some refuse, and the seller does not accept partial sale. The requirement of qiyas is that the sale is void in its entirety. But they (the Malikis) applied istihsan by upholding the contract against the seller if he accepts from those heirs who agree to take the share of those who refuse, in consideration of the interest of the heir without harming the seller due to the lack of partition.
This istihsan in this issue does not apply in Hanafi ijtihad because it is established in it that khiyar al-shart is not inherited; so if the owner of the option dies during the specified period, the ownership of the buyer in the sold item becomes final and settled if the option was for the seller, or ownership is established for the buyer's heirs if the option was for him and he died (see: al-Majalla, article 306).
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a comparative legal philosophy discussion on the concepts of istihsan and istislah. The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by distinguishing the two types of istihsan in the Hanafi school: (1) qiyasi istihsan, which is a preference for a hidden analogy over a more apparent one when multiple analogies conflict, and (2) daruri istihsan, which is a departure from qiyas due to necessity or predominent harm. The latter, he argues, is essentially a form of istislah (maslaha mursala) because it prioritizes removing hardship or harm over strict qiyas. He then contrasts this with the Maliki view, where istihsan is always a departure from qiyas in favor of an interest, and they do not consider hidden qiyas as istihsan. The key difference between istihsan and istislah, according to al-Zarqa, is that istihsan necessarily involves an exception to an existing qiyas rule, while istislah can operate in areas where there is no conflicting qiyas—it is pure interest-based legislation for public welfare. He provides examples: holding public employees liable for losses (istihsan) and imposing taxes or codifying laws (istislah). Finally, he cites al-Shatibi to show that the Maliki portrayal of istihsan still frames it as an exception, even though it falls under maslaha mursala. The footnote gives a concrete Maliki example involving khiyar al-shart inheritance, which is rejected in Hanafi law because options are not inherited.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): في هذا النص: نوعان عند الحنفية: قياسي (ترجيح قياس خفي) وضروري (عدول عن القياس للضرورة أو المصلحة).
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): الأخذ بالمصلحة المرسلة أي المصلحة التي لم يرد دليل شرعي باعتبارها أو إلغائها.
المصالح المرسلة (al-masalih al-mursala): المصالح التي لم يشهد الشارع باعتبارها ولا بإلغائها، وتكون ملائمة لمقاصد الشريعة.
القياس (al-qiyas): إلحاق مسألة لا نص فيها بمسألة ذات نص لعلة جامعة.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which type of istihsan is considered a form of istislah (maslaha mursala)?
A) al-istihsan al-qiyasi
B) al-istihsan al-daruri
C) both types
D) neither

Key Takeaway
The relationship between istihsan and istislah is that necessity-based istihsan is a subset of istislah, while qiyasi istihsan is distinct and remains a form of qiyas.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Istislah and Its Relationship with Istihsan
Explaining al-maslahah al-mursalah, its types, examples, and comparison with istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of rulings based on al-maslahah al-mursalah and distinguish between istislah and istihsan.

Core Idea
Istislah is broader than istihsan: every istihsan is istislah but not every istislah is istihsan.

In this lesson, we explore the concept of al-maslahah al-mursalah (unrestricted public interest) and its application as a subsidiary source of Islamic law. The passage by Mustafa al-Zarqa classifies rulings based on this principle and clarifies the relationship between istislah (reliance on public interest) and istihsan (juristic preference). Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying these tools in contemporary fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of rulings under istislah
The passage divides rulings built on maslahah mursalah into two categories: those concerning public administration (e.g., taxation, penalties) and those concerning partial private interests (e.g., khiyar al-shart example).
The first type does not require opposing qiyas; the interest itself is the evidence.
2
Understand the condition for istislah
Istislah does not require that the interest contradict an existing analogy. It can be the sole evidence in matters where no text or analogy exists.
This distinguishes istislah from istihsan, which always involves opposition to qiyas.
3
Analyze al-Shatibi's distinction
Al-Shatibi states that istihsan is an exception to a general rule, while istislah (maslahah mursalah) operates where no rule exists. This shows that istihsan is a subset of istislah.
Refer to the quote from al-I'tisam in the passage.
4
Examine the Maliki example and Hanafi response
The passage presents a Maliki example where khiyar al-shart is inherited, using istihsan to allow partial execution. The Hanafi school rejects this because their rule is that khiyar al-shart does not pass to heirs.
This illustrates how different schools apply istihsan based on their principles.
5
State the relationship between istislah and istihsan
The author concludes that the relationship is absolute generality and specificity: every istihsan is a form of istislah (involving opposition to qiyas), but not every istislah is istihsan.
This is the key takeaway for the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس: المصادر التبعية: الاستصلاح

١٠/٥ - أنواع الأحكام التي تبنى على نظرية المصالح المرسلة:

الأحكام التي تبنى بالاجتهاد الاستصلاحي على قاعدة المصالح المرسلة يمكن تصنيفها إلى نوعين:

النوع الأول - الأحكام التي تتعلق بشؤون الإدارة العامة المنظمة للمصالح المجتمعية، وهي التدابير التي يتوقف عليها تنظيم تلك الشؤون والمصالح العامة، وذلك كفرض الضرائب على المتقدرين عند الحاجة إلى الأعمال العامة كتجهيز الجيوش وبناء الجسور، وتخطيط الأراضي، وإحصاء النفوس وتعبيد الطرق، وإنشاء المستشفيات ودور العجزة، وسائر ذلك.

النوع الثاني - الأحكام التي تبنى على رعاية مصلحة حقوقية جزئية (١).

أما المصلحة المرسلة التي يبنى عليها الاستصلاح فلا يشترط أن يكون فيها مخالفة لقياس يعارضها. فقد يكون الحكم الثابت بها في الشؤون العامة التي لا يوجد في الشريعة دليل على خلافها بل المصلحة فيها هي الدليل الوحيد كفرض الضرائب الإلزامية عند الحاجة وتحديد عقوبات الجرائم منعاً لفوضى الأحكام باختلاف أنظار الحكام وكإلزام الحكومة الناس بالقضاء بمذهب فقهي مخصوص، أو تقنين أحكام مدنية مختارة من عدة مذاهب فقهية اجتهادية معتبرة، لكي يعلم الناس مصير معاملاتهم وعقودهم، ويعرفوا وجهة القضاء فيها سلفاً بصورة ثابتة مستقرة.

فكل ذلك وأمثاله مما تتجدد الحاجات فيه ولا تحصى، ولا يوجد في الشريعة دلائل قياسية كانت تقتضي خلافه، إنما هو استصلاح محض. يقول الإمام أبو إسحاق الشاطبي في كتابه "الاعتصام" بعد ذكره مسائل من صور الاستحسان عند الإمام مالك:

فإن قيل: هذا من باب المصالح المرسلة لا من باب الاستحسان؟ قلنا: نعم، إلا أنهم صوروا الاستحسان تصوير الاستثناء من القواعد؛ بخلاف المصالح المرسلة اهـ (الاعتصام، ٢/٣٢٤).

(١) من الأمثلة التي يذكرها المالكية للاستحسان المبني على رعاية مصلحة جزئية ما لو اشترى أحد شيئاً على أنه بالخيار في إبرام العقد وفسخه خلال مدة معينة - وهو المسمى خيار الشرط - ثم مات المشتري قبل انقضاء المدة المضروبة، فاختلف ورثته: فرضي بعضهم بإبرام العقد ورفض بعضهم، ولم يقبل البائع تجزئة المبيع. فمقتضى القياس أن يبطل البيع في الكل. ولكنهم استحسنوا إمضاء العقد على البائع إذا قبل من رضي من الورثة بأن يأخذ حصة من رفض وذلك رعاية لمصلحة الوارث دون أن يتضرر البائع لانتفاء التجزئة. وهذا الاستحسان في هذه المسألة لا يجري في الاجتهاد الحنفي لأن من المقرر فيه أن خيار الشرط لا يورث فيموت صاحب الخيار خلال المدة المضروبة ينبرم ويستقر ملك المشتري في المبيع نهائياً إن كان الخيار للبائع، أو يثبت الملك فيه لورثة المشتري إن كان الخيار له فمات (ر: المجلة / ٣٠٦).

ويقول الأستاذ الشيخ محمد أبو زهرة في كتابه "مالك" تعليقاً على عبارة الشاطبي هذه:

ومعنى هذا الكلام أن الاستحسان استثناء جزئي في مقابل دليل كلي يتخلف في بعض الأجزاء. أما المصالح المرسلة فإنها تكون حيث لا يكون ثمة دليل سواها. وإنا نجد أن إيثار المصلحة الجزئية - أي الاستحسان - هو هو بلا ريب أخذ بمبدأ المصالح المرسلة. ولذلك يقول علماء المالكية إن الاستحسان هو إيثار للاستدلال المرسل - أي للاستدلال بالمصلحة المرسلة - على الاستدلال بالقياس.

ومؤدى ذلك أن المصلحة تعمل في حالين:

(الحالة الأولى) - حيث لا يكون في الموضوع قياس محمول على نص. وحينئذ تكون المصلحة هي الدليل وحدها وهي عند مالك أصل مستقل قائم بذاته.

(الحالة الثانية) - إذا كان ثمة قياس يوقع اطراده في مشقة أو ضيق، أو ينافي مصلحة ظاهرة. فحينئذ يترخص في ترك القياس لهذا النفع المجتلب، ولذلك الضرر هذا النوع الذي قوبل بالقياس استحساناً. اهـ عن كتاب "مالك" القسم الثاني ف/ ١٧٩/ باختصار وتصرف يسير.

أقول:

يتضح مما تقدم أن النسبة بين الاستحسان والاستصلاح هي من قبيل العموم والخصوص المطلق. فالاستصلاح أعم، والاستحسان أخص. فكل استحسان هو وجه من الاستصلاح خولف فيه القياس، وليس كل ما بني من الأحكام على قاعدة المصالح المرسلة يعد استحساناً، لأن الاستحسان هو الطريق الذي فيه مخالفة للقواعد القياسية.
Chapter Five: Subsidiary Sources: Istislah

10/5 - Types of rulings built on the theory of public interests:

The rulings built by istislahi ijtihad on the basis of al-maslahah al-mursalah can be classified into two types:

The first type - rulings related to public administration affairs organizing societal interests, which are measures upon which the organization of those affairs and public interests depends, such as imposing taxes on the wealthy when there is need for public works like equipping armies, building bridges, land planning, census, paving roads, establishing hospitals, nursing homes, and so on.

The second type - rulings built on the consideration of a partial individual interest (1).

As for al-maslahah al-mursalah upon which istislah is based, it is not required that it involve opposition to an analogy that contradicts it. Because the ruling established by it in public affairs where there is no evidence in the Sharia to the contrary, rather the interest is the only evidence, such as imposing compulsory taxes when needed, specifying penalties for crimes to prevent chaos of rulings due to different views of judges, and obligating the government to judge by a particular juristic school, or codifying civil laws selected from several valid juristic schools, so that people know the fate of their transactions and contracts, and know in advance the direction of judgment in them in a fixed and stable manner.

All that and its like, where needs are renewed and countless, and there is no analogical evidence in the Sharia that would necessitate the opposite, it is pure istislah. Imam Abu Ishaq al-Shatibi said in his book "al-I'tisam" after mentioning issues of istihsan according to Imam Malik:

If it is said: This belongs to the chapter of al-masalih al-mursalah, not to the chapter of istihsan? We say: Yes, except that they depicted istihsan as a depiction of an exception from the rules; unlike al-masalih al-mursalah. End quote (al-I'tisam, 2/324).

(1) Among the examples that the Malikis mention for istihsan based on considering a partial interest is: if someone bought something with the condition that he has the option to conclude the contract or cancel it within a specified period — which is called khiyar al-shart — then the buyer died before the expiry of the specified period, and his heirs disagreed: some agreed to conclude the contract and some refused, and the seller did not accept partial sale. The requirement of analogy is that the sale is void in its entirety. But they (the Malikis) deemed it good (istihsan) to execute the contract on the seller if he accepts from those heirs who agreed to take the share of those who refused, taking care of the interest of the heir without harming the seller due to the absence of partial sale. This istihsan in this issue does not apply in Hanafi ijtihad because it is established therein that khiyar al-shart is not inherited, so if the owner of the option dies during the specified period, the ownership of the buyer in the sold item becomes final if the option was for the seller, or ownership is established for the buyer's heirs if the option was for him and he died (ref: Majallah / 306).

Shaykh Muhammad Abu Zahra said in his book "Malik" commenting on this statement of al-Shatibi:

The meaning of this speech is that istihsan is a partial exception in return for a universal evidence that falls short in some parts. As for al-masalih al-mursalah, they occur where there is no evidence other than them. And we find that preferring the partial interest — i.e., istihsan — is undoubtedly taking the principle of al-masalih al-mursalah. Therefore, Maliki scholars say that istihsan is preferring the mursal evidence — i.e., reasoning by al-maslahah al-mursalah — over reasoning by analogy.

The consequence is that interest operates in two cases:

(First case) - Where there is no analogy based on a text in the matter. Then the interest is the only evidence, and with Malik it is an independent original source.

(Second case) - If there is an analogy whose application would cause hardship or difficulty, or conflict with an apparent interest. Then one may relax in leaving the analogy for this derived benefit, and this type that is confronted with analogy is called istihsan. End quote from the book "Malik" section two, p. 179, abridged and slightly modified.

I say:

It becomes clear from the above that the relationship between istihsan and istislah is that of absolute generality and specificity. Istislah is more general, and istihsan is more specific. Every istihsan is a form of istislah in which analogy has been opposed, and not every ruling built on the basis of al-masalih al-mursalah is considered istihsan, because istihsan is the method that involves opposition to analogical rules.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy exposition on the concept of al-maslahah al-mursalah (unrestricted public interest) and its application in Islamic jurisprudence. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, classifies rulings based on this principle into two types: those governing public administration (e.g., taxation, penalties, codification) and those concerning partial private interests. He emphasizes that istislah does not necessarily require opposition to qiyas (analogy); it can be the sole evidence where no text or analogy exists. The text then quotes al-Shatibi to distinguish istihsan (juristic preference) from istislah: istihsan is an exception to general rules, whereas istislah operates where no rule exists. A Maliki example involving khiyar al-shart and inheritance illustrates istihsan, but the author notes that in the Hanafi school, khiyar al-shart does not pass to heirs, so the Maliki solution does not apply. Finally, Abu Zahra and the author clarify that istislah is broader than istihsan: every istihsan is a type of istislah that opposes qiyas, but istislah can also operate without any conflicting qiyas.

Key Terms
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): مصدر تبعي يبنى على المصلحة المرسلة، سواء خالف القياس أم لا، ويشمل الاستحسان وغيره (subsidiary source based on unrestricted interest, whether or not it contradicts analogy; includes istihsan and others)
المصلحة المرسلة (al-maslahah al-mursalah): مصلحة لم ينص الشارع على اعتبارها ولا على إلغائها، وهي الدليل الوحيد في بعض الأحكام العامة (interest not specifically recognized or invalidated by the Lawgiver; the sole evidence in some public rulings)
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): العدول بالمسألة عن نظائرها إلى حكم آخر لمصلحة، وهو أخص من الاستصلاح (departing from the analogous ruling to another for the sake of interest; more specific than istislah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between istislah and istihsan?
A) They are synonymous terms for the same concept.
B) Istihsan is broader and includes istislah.
C) Istislah is broader and includes istihsan.
D) They are completely unrelated concepts.

Key Takeaway
Istislah (based on maslahah mursalah) is a broader subsidiary source than istihsan; the latter is a specific type that involves setting aside analogy for a stronger interest.

Types of Rulings Based on Masalih Mursalah
Classification of rulings from Masalih Mursalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify rulings derived from Masalih Mursalah and apply the Hanafi position (istihsan) to distinguish from the Maliki approach.

Core Idea
Masalih Mursalah allow public welfare rulings, but Hanafis prefer istihsan to ensure Shari'ah consistency.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction to Fiqh classifies the types of rulings that can be built on the theory of Masalih Mursalah. It also clarifies the relationship between istihsan and istislah, a crucial distinction for Hanafi jurists. Through this lesson, you will learn how public welfare needs are addressed within the Hanafi framework, using istihsan as the primary tool rather than adopting Mursalah as an independent source.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Locate the two categories of rulings: Type One (public administration) and Type Two (judicial system and private rights). Notice that the passage focuses on Type One with examples.
Type Two is only mentioned; examples are promised later.
2
Understand Hanafi vs Maliki on Mursalah
Read the Abu Zahra quote and the author's conclusion that istihsan is a subset of istislah. For Hanafis, Mursalah is not an independent source; they use istihsan to achieve similar ends.
This distinction affects how a Hanafi mufti derives rulings for novel issues.
3
Analyze the examples
Examine the examples: taxation for public works, price controls, traffic regulation, and Umar's Diwan. Identify how each serves a public interest without contradicting a clear text.
Note the condition: must align with Shari'ah objectives (maqasid).
4
Apply the conditions
For a ruling based on Masalih Mursalah, verify it does not conflict with a clear text, ijma, or clear qiyas. If it does conflict, istihsan may still apply if there is a stronger need.
Hanafis require that the interest be genuine and necessary (daruri or haji).
5
Role of the ruler
Recognize that the supreme ruler (caliph or equivalent) has authority to enact such regulations. The examples show that these are not individual ijtihad but state-level decisions.
This connects to the concept of siyasah shar'iyyah.

10/5 - Types of rulings built on the theory of Mursalah interests:
Rulings built by istislahi ijtihad on the basis of Mursalah interests can be classified into two types:
Type One - Rulings related to public administration affairs that organize community interests, which are measures on whose organization depends those affairs and public interests, such as imposing taxes on the affluent when there is need for public works like equipping armies, building bridges, land planning, census taking, paving roads, establishing hospitals and nursing homes, and all
(1) Professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra says in his book (Malik) commenting on al-Shatibi's statement:
"The meaning of this statement is that istihsan is a partial exception in contrast to a universal evidence that fails in some parts. As for Mursalah interests, they occur where there is no evidence other than them. Indeed, we find that preferring partial interest - i.e., istihsan - is undoubtedly an adoption of the principle of Mursalah interests. Therefore, Maliki scholars say that istihsan is preferring the Mursalah inference - i.e., inference by Mursalah interest - over inference by analogy.
The implication is that interest operates in two situations:
(First situation) - Where there is no analogy carried on a text. Then interest is the sole evidence, and with Malik it is an independent principle in itself. (Second situation) - If there is an analogy whose application leads to hardship or constraint, or contradicts an apparent interest. Then one may be lenient by leaving the analogy for this attracted benefit, and this type that was met with analogy is called istihsan." End quote from the book "Malik" Part Two, p. 179, with slight abridgment and modification.
Al-mujtanab, and it was called
I say:
It becomes clear from the above that the relationship between istihsan and istislah is that of absolute generality and specificity.
So istislah is more general, and istihsan is more specific. Thus every istihsan is a form of istislah in which analogy is contravened, but not every ruling built on the basis of Mursalah interests is considered istihsan, because istihsan is the method that involves deviation from analogical rules.

Chapter (5) Subordinate Sources: Istislah
Collective security that eliminates poverty, guarantees work for those who want it, and achieves for all people a decent standard of living and essential facilities for their lives. All such vital interests and their like, which are known and needed, or will arise and be needed by public life, must be cooperated upon by members of society and financed according to each one's ability, in accordance with the commands of the Shari'ah, whose Qur'an says: "And cooperate in righteousness and piety, but do not cooperate in sin and aggression" (Al-Ma'idah 5:2).
Establishing the institutions necessary for each such project, and financing those public institutions through fair taxes and levies according to need, are istislahi rulings.
Of this kind is price control with its Shari'ah conditions, so that sellers do not become tyrannical and control people's needs.
Also of this kind is what 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, the second Caliph (may Allah be pleased with him), initiated: establishing the Diwan to regulate the stipends of soldiers, their salaries, duration of service, etc. Then the Diwans were generalized for other interests.
Also of this kind in our present era is traffic regulation on internal and external roads with special systems after the advent of motor vehicles, to prevent running over and collisions, and to protect people's lives.
All that is a requirement of the general authority of the supreme ruler in the state, whether a Caliph or other; for he has the right to organize conditions, establish institutions, enact necessary regulations for creating, financing, and managing these works, and impose penalties on violators according to emerging needs, all in accordance with the rules and objectives of the Shari'ah.
Type Two - Rulings related to the judicial system and private rights.
And you will see real examples in both subjects:

Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a classification of rulings based on the theory of Masalih Mursalah (unrestricted public interests). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, divides such rulings into two types. The first type concerns public administration and community welfare, such as taxation for public works, price controls, and modern traffic regulation. The second type concerns the judicial system and private rights, which he promises to illustrate later. The passage also includes a detailed commentary by Muhammad Abu Zahra on the relationship between istihsan and istislah, explaining that istihsan is a subset of istislah where a specific interest is preferred over analogy. For Hanafis, istihsan is a recognized method, but Masalih Mursalah as an independent source is less emphasized; they often use istihsan to achieve similar ends. The examples from Umar's establishment of the Diwan and contemporary traffic laws demonstrate how rulers can enact regulations for public benefit, as long as they align with Shari'ah objectives.

Key Terms
المصالح المرسلة (al-masalih al-mursalah): Public interests not directly addressed by a specific textual evidence, but considered valid for legislation because they align with Shari'ah objectives.
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): Method of deriving rulings based on the principle of Masalih Mursalah, i.e., considering public welfare as a basis for legislation.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Preferring a stronger evidence over analogy, or deviating from an analogical ruling in favor of a more suitable one based on public interest, necessity, or custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two types of rulings based on Masalih Mursalah?
A) Type one: rulings related to public administration; Type two: rulings related to judicial system and private rights
B) Type one: rulings related to worship; Type two: rulings related to transactions
C) Type one: rulings based on Quran; Type two: rulings based on Sunnah
D) Type one: rulings that follow analogy; Type two: rulings that follow istihsan

Key Takeaway
Rulings based on Masalih Mursalah are valid in the Hanafi school through istihsan, provided they serve genuine public interest without contradicting Shari'ah texts or principles.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Scope of Istislah: Collective Welfare and the Ruler's Authority
Introduction to istislah with basis and early examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the basis of istislah in the Quran and Sunnah, and evaluate its application under the ruler's authority within the constraints of Shari'ah principles.

Core Idea
Istislah derives from collective obligation (fard kifayah) to secure welfare, but its rulings must align with Shari'ah objectives.

This lesson examines the concept of istislah as presented by Mustafa al-Zarqa. The passage emphasizes that social welfare—eliminating poverty, providing work, and ensuring basic needs—is a collective responsibility mandated by the Quran (5:2). The ruler, by virtue of his general authority, may enact regulations such as price controls, establish institutions (e.g., the Diwan of Umar), and implement modern traffic laws, all within the framework of Shari'ah. These are not independent legislation but subordinate to revealed principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shari'ah basis for collective welfare
Examine Quran 5:2 cited in the passage: 'Cooperate in righteousness and piety.' This establishes a duty to secure public interests. Recognize how the Hanafi school interprets this as a source for istislah, but only when consistent with nass and ijma.
The verse is general; its application to specific measures requires juristic reasoning (ijtihad).
2
Analyze the historical precedents
Review the examples: Umar's Diwan (soldiers' stipends) and price regulation. Understand that these were novel measures justified by necessity (maslahah) and not contradicting primary texts.
The passage highlights that the ruler's authority is 'according to the rules and objectives of the Shari'ah'.
3
Apply the criteria to modern regulations
Consider the modern example of traffic laws. Discuss how these regulations protect lives (hifz al-nafs) and are maslahah mursalah. Check against Shari'ah: no explicit text prohibits them, and they serve a clear benefit.
Hanafis would categorize this as istihsan or siyasah shar'iyyah, depending on the mechanism.

Chapter (5): Secondary Sources: Istislah (Consideration of Public Interest). The collective guarantee that eliminates misery, secures work for those who want it, and achieves for all people a decent standard of living and essential facilities for their lives. All these vital interests and their like, which are known and needed, or will arise and be needed by public life, must be cooperated upon by the members of society and funded according to each person's ability, in accordance with the orders of the Shari'ah, whose Quran says: 'And cooperate in righteousness and piety, but do not cooperate in sin and aggression' (Al-Ma'idah 5:2). The establishment of necessary institutions for every project of this kind, and the funding of those public institutions through taxes and fair collection according to need, are istislahi rulings. Of this kind is price regulation with its Shari conditions, so that sellers do not become tyrannical and control people's needs. Also of this kind is what Umar ibn al-Khattab, the second caliph (may Allah be pleased with him), introduced in establishing the Diwan to regulate soldiers' stipends, salaries, and length of service, etc. Then the Diwans were generalized for other interests. Also of this kind in our present age is traffic regulation on internal and external roads with special systems after the advent of motor vehicles, to prevent trampling and collisions, and to protect people's lives. All of this is a requirement of the general authority of the supreme power holder in the state, whether a caliph or other; for he has the right to organize conditions, establish institutions, enact necessary systems for creating these works, funding them, and managing them, and to impose penalties on violators according to what new exigencies he finds, all in accordance with the rules and objectives of the Shari'ah. The second type: rulings related to the judicial system and private rights. And here are the actual examples in both subjects: First: regarding the judicial system. Examples of this type include the following measures and rulings: 1) Specialization of the judiciary by subject matter, so that each type of claim has a specialized court that hears it and is prohibited from hearing others; such as criminal courts for trying criminals and civil courts for hearing financial claims; and so forth of specialized courts according to temporal exigencies, the abundance of claims, and systems' need for specialization in knowledge and distribution of work. 2) Making the judiciary and courts in degrees (two or three) so that the party against whom a judgment is rendered has a way to appeal the decision against him before a higher court that can annul or modify it if it finds injustice or error. 3) Preventing judges from hearing claims regarding an old right whose owner neglected to claim it for a long specified period without excuse, due to suspicion at that time about the original right and its proof after lapse of time, and to free the judiciary from confusion in digging up old events, and to compel people to pursue their rights and not neglect them. This judicial measure, known as the issue of prescription or the matter of passage of time, was approved by later jurists and was implemented by sultanic orders in the Ottoman era (see f 6/15). Second: regarding private rights. Examples of this type include the following cases: 1) The ruling of dissolution of marriage between a missing person (1) and his wife upon her request, even if his death has not been proven, after four years have passed since his disappearance in the case... (1) The missing person is one whose news has been cut off and his location is unknown, and it is not known whether he is alive or dead. If his life is known, then he is absent, not missing.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work discusses al-maslahah al-mursalah (consideration of public interest) as a secondary source of Shari'ah. The author begins by emphasizing collective societal responsibility for public welfare, citing Quran 5:2. He then provides examples from both early Islamic history (Caliph Umar's establishment of the Diwan) and contemporary times (traffic regulation) to illustrate that such regulations are valid as long as they align with Shari'ah principles. The passage then categorizes rulings into two types: those related to judicial system and those related to private rights. Under the judicial system, three examples are given: specialization of courts, appellate system, and statute of limitations (prescription). The last example of private rights concerns dissolution of marriage for a missing person after four years, with a footnote defining al-mafqud. The author stresses that all these measures fall under the general authority of the ruler, who may enact necessary regulations consistent with maqasid al-shari'ah. This passage shows how later Hanafi jurists (متأخرو الفقهاء) expanded the scope of istislah in areas not explicitly covered by primary sources, as seen in the Ottoman adoption of the statute of limitations.

Key Terms
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): A secondary source of law derived from consideration of public interest (maslaha) not explicitly legislated in primary texts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary Shari'ah justification for istislah rulings like establishing the Diwan?
A) The explicit command in the Quran to establish a Diwan.
B) The general command to cooperate in righteousness (Quran 5:2) and the ruler's authority to regulate welfare.
C) The practice of the Prophet ﷺ who instituted the Diwan.
D) The consensus of all companions.

Key Takeaway
Istislah is a valid secondary source in the Hanafi school when applied within the constraints of Shari'ah principles and objectives, especially under the ruler's authority.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

Istislah in the Judicial System: Specialization, Appeals, and Prescription
Examples of istislah in the judicial system

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Hanafi jurists use istislah to justify judicial reforms such as court specialization, appellate courts, and statutes of limitations.

Core Idea
Judicial reforms like specialization and prescription are istislahi measures that serve maqasid without contradicting nass.

This lesson focuses on the second part of the passage, where al-Zarqa provides three examples of istislah in the judicial system: specialized courts, appellate hierarchy, and the statute of limitations (prescription). These measures, not explicitly found in primary sources, were adopted by later Hanafi jurists to meet changing needs. The passage stresses that they are valid under the ruler's authority and consistent with Shari'ah objectives, such as justice and efficiency.

Guided Path
1
Understand the need for judicial specialization
The passage notes that as claims increased, courts were organized by subject (criminal, civil). This is a practical necessity to ensure proper adjudication. Recognize that no explicit text mandates this division; it is an istislah ruling to achieve justice.
Specialization is a means to better implement Shari'ah, not a change in substantive law.
2
Analyze the appellate system
The passage describes a two- or three-tier system allowing appeals to higher courts. This provides a mechanism to correct errors and injustices. Evaluate how this serves the maqsad of justice (adl).
The right to appeal is not mentioned in classical fiqh; it emerged from necessity.
3
Examine the statute of limitations (prescription)
The passage explains that after a long period without claim, the claim is barred. Reasons: suspicion about the original right, avoiding digging up old events, and encouraging pursuit of rights. Note that later Hanafi jurists (muta'akhkhirun) approved this, and it was enacted in Ottoman law.
Key term: al-taqadum. Early Hanafis generally rejected prescription except for property; this is an extension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٥) المصادر التبعية الاستصلاح
الضمان الجماعي الذي ينفي البؤس، ويكفل العمل لمن يريده ويحقق وجوه
لجميع الناس المستوى اللائق في المعيشة والمرافق الضرورية لحياتهم. فكل تلك المصالح الحيوية وأمثالها مما هو معروف ومحتاج إليه، أو سيتولد وتحتاج إليه الحياة العامة، يجب أن يتعاون عليه أفراد المجتمع ويمولوه بحسب قدرة كل منهم وفقاً لأوامر الشريعة التي يقول قرآنها : ﴿وَتَعَاوَنُوا عَلَى الْبِرِّ وَالتَّقْوَى وَلَا تَعَاوَنُوا عَلَى الْإِثْمِ وَالْعُدْوَانِ﴾ (المائدة : ٢/٥)
فإنشاء المؤسسات اللازمة لكل مشروع من هذا القبيل، وتمويل تلك المؤسسات العامة بالضرائب والجباية العادلة بحسب الحاجة، هو أحكام استصلاحية .
من هذا القبيل تحديد الأسعار بشروطه الشرعية، كي لا يطغى الباعة فيها ويتحكموا بحوائج الناس.
من هذا القبيل أيضاً ما أحدثه عمر بن الخطاب الخليفة الثاني رضي الله عنه من إنشاء الديوان لضبط عطاء الجند وأرزاقهم ومدة خدمتهم إلخ ... ثم عممت الدواوين في مصالح أخرى.
ومن هذا القبيل أيضاً في عصرنا الحاضر تنظيم السير في الطرق الداخلية والخارجية بأنظمة خاصة بعد حدوث السيارات الآلية، منعاً للدعس
والاصطدام، وصيانة لأرواح الناس.
وكل ذلك هو من مقتضى الولاية العامة لصاحب السلطة العليا في الدولة من خليفة أو غيره ؛ فإنَّ له أن ينظم الأوضاع، ويؤسس المؤسسات ويسن الأنظمة اللازمة لإنشاء هذه الأعمال وتمويلها وإدارتها وأن يفرض العقوبات على المخالفين بحسب ما يجد من الدواعي الجديدة، كل ذلك وفقاً لقواعد الشريعة ومقاصدها.
النوع الثاني - الأحكام التي تتعلق بالنظام القضائي والحقوق الخاصة.
وإليك الأمثلة الواقعية في كلا الموضوعين :
أولاً: في النظام القضائي.
من الأمثلة الواقعة على هذا النوع التدابير والأحكام التالية :
١ - تخصيص القضاء من حيث الموضوع بحيث يكون لكل نوع من الدعاوي محكمة مختصة تنظر فيه ويمتنع عليها النظر في سواه ؛ كالمحاكم الجنائية لمحاكمة المجرمين والمحاكم الحقوقية للنظر في دعاوي الحقوق المالية؛ إلى غير ذلك من المحاكم المختصة بحسب الدواعي الزمنية، وكثرة الدعاوي، والأنظمة وحاجتها إلى الاختصاص في العلم والاختصاص في توزيع العمل .
٢ - جعل القضاء والمحاكم على درجات (اثنتين أو ثلاث) بحيث يكون أمام المحكوم عليه طريق للطعن في الحكم الصادر عليه أمام محكمة أعلى تستطيع فسخه أو تعديله إذا وجدت فيه جوراً أو خطأ.
٣ - منع القضاة من سماع الدعاوي بحق قديم أهمل صاحبه الادعاء به زمناً طويلاً معيناً بلا عذر، وذلك للشك عندئذ في أصل الحق وفي إثباته بعد التقادم، ولتخليص القضاء من الارتباك في نبش الوقائع القديمة، ولحمل الناس على متابعة حقوقهم وعدم إهمالها. وهذا التدبير القضائي المعروف بقضية التقادم أو مسألة مرور الزمان التي أقرها متأخرو الفقهاء، وصَدّرت بها الأوامر السلطانية في العهد العثماني (ر: ف ٦/١٥).
ثانياً: في الحقوق الخاصة.
من أمثلة هذا النوع القضايا التالية :
١ - الحكم بانحلال الزواج بين المفقود (١) وزوجته بناء على طلبها وإن لم يثبت موته، وذلك بعد مضي أربع سنوات على فقدانه في حال
(١) المفقود هو الشخص الذي انقطعت أخباره ولا يعلم له مكان، ولا يعرف هل هو حي أو ميت . فإذا كان معلوم الحياة فهو عندئذ غائب لا مفقود.
Chapter (5): Secondary Sources: Istislah (Consideration of Public Interest). The collective guarantee that eliminates misery, secures work for those who want it, and achieves for all people a decent standard of living and essential facilities for their lives. All these vital interests and their like, which are known and needed, or will arise and be needed by public life, must be cooperated upon by the members of society and funded according to each person's ability, in accordance with the orders of the Shari'ah, whose Quran says: 'And cooperate in righteousness and piety, but do not cooperate in sin and aggression' (Al-Ma'idah 5:2). The establishment of necessary institutions for every project of this kind, and the funding of those public institutions through taxes and fair collection according to need, are istislahi rulings. Of this kind is price regulation with its Shari conditions, so that sellers do not become tyrannical and control people's needs. Also of this kind is what Umar ibn al-Khattab, the second caliph (may Allah be pleased with him), introduced in establishing the Diwan to regulate soldiers' stipends, salaries, and length of service, etc. Then the Diwans were generalized for other interests. Also of this kind in our present age is traffic regulation on internal and external roads with special systems after the advent of motor vehicles, to prevent trampling and collisions, and to protect people's lives. All of this is a requirement of the general authority of the supreme power holder in the state, whether a caliph or other; for he has the right to organize conditions, establish institutions, enact necessary systems for creating these works, funding them, and managing them, and to impose penalties on violators according to what new exigencies he finds, all in accordance with the rules and objectives of the Shari'ah. The second type: rulings related to the judicial system and private rights. And here are the actual examples in both subjects: First: regarding the judicial system. Examples of this type include the following measures and rulings: 1) Specialization of the judiciary by subject matter, so that each type of claim has a specialized court that hears it and is prohibited from hearing others; such as criminal courts for trying criminals and civil courts for hearing financial claims; and so forth of specialized courts according to temporal exigencies, the abundance of claims, and systems' need for specialization in knowledge and distribution of work. 2) Making the judiciary and courts in degrees (two or three) so that the party against whom a judgment is rendered has a way to appeal the decision against him before a higher court that can annul or modify it if it finds injustice or error. 3) Preventing judges from hearing claims regarding an old right whose owner neglected to claim it for a long specified period without excuse, due to suspicion at that time about the original right and its proof after lapse of time, and to free the judiciary from confusion in digging up old events, and to compel people to pursue their rights and not neglect them. This judicial measure, known as the issue of prescription or the matter of passage of time, was approved by later jurists and was implemented by sultanic orders in the Ottoman era (see f 6/15). Second: regarding private rights. Examples of this type include the following cases: 1) The ruling of dissolution of marriage between a missing person (1) and his wife upon her request, even if his death has not been proven, after four years have passed since his disappearance in the case... (1) The missing person is one whose news has been cut off and his location is unknown, and it is not known whether he is alive or dead. If his life is known, then he is absent, not missing.

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work discusses al-maslahah al-mursalah (consideration of public interest) as a secondary source of Shari'ah. Key Terms
التقادم (al-taqadum): Statute of limitations; barring claims due to passage of time without valid excuse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the Hanafi position on prescription as presented in the passage?
A) It is rejected in all cases as a bid'ah.
B) It is accepted by later jurists and implemented by Ottoman sultanic orders to serve public interest.
C) It is accepted only for criminal cases.
D) It is accepted only for immovable property, as always.

Key Takeaway
Judicial reforms like specialization, appeals, and prescription are valid istislahi measures, anchored by later Hanafi jurists in public interest and consistent with Shari'ah objectives.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Istislah in Family Law: The Case of the Missing Person (Al-Mafqud)
Example of istislah in private rights: missing person's marriage dissolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a fatwa on the dissolution of marriage for a missing person's wife using the Hanafi istislah framework.

Core Idea
The wife of a missing person may obtain a judicial divorce after four years—a ruling based on istislah, not explicit text.

This lesson focuses on the final example in the passage: the dissolution of marriage for a missing person (al-mafqud) at his wife's request after four years of disappearance, even without proof of death. This ruling, not found in the early Hanafi works, was adopted by later jurists based on necessity and public interest. It illustrates how istislah operates in private family law to prevent prolonged hardship.

Guided Path
1
Define the missing person (al-mafqud)
Read the footnote: a person whose whereabouts and life status are unknown. Distinguish from gha'ib (absent but known alive). This definition is crucial for applying the ruling.
If the person is known to be alive, he is gha'ib, and the wife cannot seek divorce based on this rule.
2
Identify the harm and the remedy
The wife faces indefinite suspension without marital rights. The remedy is dissolution after a specified period (4 years). The passage implies this is a judicial process.
This is a case of pressing necessity (darurah) that justifies departure from the normal rule that marriage continues until death or valid divorce.
3
Trace the istislah reasoning
No explicit text sets a time limit for such cases. The four-year period is an ijtihad based on analogy (qiyas) to the waiting period of a widow (four months and ten days?) but extended due to the unique hardship. The later jurists deemed it an istislah ruling.
The passage does not detail the evidence; it simply presents the ruling as an example of istislah in private rights.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work discusses al-maslahah al-mursalah (consideration of public interest) as a secondary source of Shari'ah. The author begins by emphasizing collective societal responsibility for public welfare, citing Quran 5:2. He then provides examples from both early Islamic history (Caliph Umar's establishment of the Diwan) and contemporary times (traffic regulation) to illustrate that such regulations are valid as long as they align with Shari'ah principles. The passage then categorizes rulings into two types: those related to judicial system and those related to private rights. Under the judicial system, three examples are given: specialization of courts, appellate system, and statute of limitations (prescription). The last example of private rights concerns dissolution of marriage for a missing person after four years, with a footnote defining al-mafqud. The author stresses that all these measures fall under the general authority of the ruler, who may enact necessary regulations consistent with maqasid al-shari'ah. This passage shows how later Hanafi jurists (متأخرو الفقهاء) expanded the scope of istislah in areas not explicitly covered by primary sources, as seen in the Ottoman adoption of the statute of limitations.

Key Terms
المفقود (al-mafqud): A person whose news has ceased and whose whereabouts are unknown, with no knowledge of whether he is alive or dead.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The wife must wait until she reaches the age of menopause.
C) Four years must have passed since the husband's disappearance, and the wife must request it.
D) The husband's property must be distributed first.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on the missing person's wife exemplifies how istislah produces a specific, time-bound solution to a persistent legal dilemma.

Judicial Measures Based on Istislah: Three Examples
Three judicial istislah-based measures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three judicial measures derived from istislah and explain how each serves public interest within the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
Istislah justifies judicial reforms like court specialization, appeals, and statutes of limitations.

This passage presents three practical examples of how the subsidiary source of istislah (public interest) has been applied in Islamic judicial history. These measures—court specialization, appellate review, and prescription of claims—were adopted by later Hanafi jurists and Ottoman authorities to ensure justice and efficiency. Understanding these examples deepens your grasp of how istislah operates beyond theoretical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify each example
List the three measures: specialization of courts by subject, multi-level appellate system, and statute of limitations for stale claims.
Each measure addresses a specific judicial challenge.
2
Understand the maslahah
For each measure, identify the public interest it serves: efficiency and expertise (specialization), error correction and fairness (appeals), and stability and finality (prescription).
Istislah is the basis for these measures.
3
Note the authority level
Recognize that these measures are based on istislah, approved by later Hanafi jurists (mutakhkhirin), and enforced by Ottoman sultanic orders.
This provides the fiqh hierarchy for iftaa.

۱ - تخصيص القضاء من حيث الموضوع بحيث يكون لكل نوع من الدعاوي محكمة مختصة تنظر فيه ويمتنع عليها النظر في سواه ؛ كالمحاكم الجنائية لمحاكمة المجرمين والمحاكم الحقوقية للنظر في دعاوي الحقوق المالية؛ إلى غير ذلك من المحاكم المختصة بحسب الدواعي الزمنية، وكثرة الدعاوي ،والأنظمة وحاجتها إلى الإخصاء في العلم والاختصاص في توزيع العمل .

5/11 - First: Regarding the judicial system. Examples of this type include the following measures and rulings: 1- Specialization of the judiciary by subject matter, so that each type of claim has a specialized court that hears it and is prohibited from hearing others; like criminal courts for trying criminals and civil courts for hearing financial claims; and other specialized courts according to temporal circumstances, the abundance of claims, systems and their need for specialization in knowledge and division of labor. 2- Making the judiciary and courts in two or three degrees, so that the litigant has a way to appeal the judgment issued against him before a higher court that can annul or amend it if it finds injustice or error. 3- Preventing the hearing of claims regarding an old right that the claimant neglected to assert for a specified long period without excuse, due to doubt about the existence of the right and its proof after passage of time, and to free the judiciary from confusion in digging up old events, and to encourage people to pursue their rights and not neglect them. This judicial measure is known as the issue of prescription or the matter of passage of time, which was approved by later jurists and implemented by sultanic orders in the Ottoman era (see: F 6/15).

Commentary
This passage lists three examples of judicial measures based on the subsidiary source of istislah (public interest). The author presents these as applications of the principle that the ruler may enact regulations for the proper functioning of the judiciary, even if not explicitly found in primary texts. Example 1: Specialization of courts by subject matter to improve efficiency. Example 2: Appellate courts to allow review of judgments. Example 3: Statute of limitations to prevent stale claims, accepted by later Hanafi scholars and Ottoman imperial orders. These measures are driven by the need for justice and avoidance of hardship.

Key Terms
الاستصلاح (al-istiṣlāḥ): Public interest; a subsidiary source of law used when primary texts are silent and the measure serves a clear maslahah without contradicting explicit rulings.
التقادم (al-taqādum): Prescription or statute of limitations; the legal bar that prevents hearing claims after a specified period of neglect without excuse.
محكمة مختصة (maḥkama mukhtaṣṣa): Specialized court; a court with jurisdiction over a specific subject matter (e.g., criminal, civil).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Istislah allows the ruler to implement judicial measures like court specialization, appeals, and statutes of limitations to serve public interest, as approved by later Hanafi jurists and Ottoman practice.

Istislah Overriding Qiyas: The Missing Husband Case
Applying istislah to permit a wife of a missing husband to remarry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the application of istislah to override qiyas in the case of a missing husband, and identify the practical difference between Hanafi istishab and Maliki istislah.

Core Idea
Istislah can override strict qiyas to prevent severe harm, even though istishab presumes continuity.

This passage examines a classic legal problem: the wife of a missing husband. The Hanafi school adheres to istishab, keeping the marriage intact indefinitely. The Maliki school, following a judgment of Umar, uses istislah to allow her to remarry. The author argues that strict analogy between financial and marital rights is flawed, and that public interest demands flexibility. Understanding this tension is crucial for a mufti who must balance textual principles with maqasid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing legal principles
Recognize that the Hanafi position relies on istishab (presumption of continuity), while the Maliki position relies on istislah (public interest). The author contrasts these two foundations.
Istishab: 'original state continues until evidence to the contrary.'
2
Analyze the fundamental difference between rights
The author argues that marital rights have different considerations than financial rights. Indefinite detention of wealth causes no major harm, but indefinite suspension of a wife's marital status causes great harm and may lead to corruption.
Qiyas comparing marriage to wealth is flawed because the illa (reasoning) differs.
3
Understand the historical application
Umar ibn al-Khattab judged that the wife could remarry. The Maliki school adopted this, and it influenced Ottoman and Egyptian family law. This shows istislah in practice.
The text mentions Ottoman Family Law Code and Egyptian Law 25 of 1920.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school did not adopt this ruling; they insisted on istishab until death is proven. However, the author suggests that istihsan/istislah could be used to avoid hardship, implying a possible opening within Hanafi methodology.
No explicit Hanafi rebuttal is given; the author presents the problem and advocates for flexibility.
5
Derive the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Hanafi, the wife remains married indefinitely, unable to remarry. Under Maliki (and Ottoman/Egyptian law), she can remarry after judicial dissolution and iddah. This is the key practical difference.
The mufta bihi in many modern Muslim family codes follows the Maliki view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٥) المصادر التبعية الاستصلاح
مسألة المفقود: إذا فقد الزوج في حرب أو نحوه ولم يعلم خبره، فعندئذ يسوغ لزوجته أن تتزوج من غيره بعد أن يقضي القاضي بانحلال الزوجية وتمضي الزوجة عدتها الشرعية، دفعاً لضرر الزوجة من بقائها معلقة مدى العمر، ولو كان للزوج المفقود مال كاف لنفقتها. بهذا قضى عمر بن الخطاب وعليه جرى الاجتهاد المالكي تبعاً لقضاء عمر، وبه أيضاً أخذ قانون حقوق العائلة الصادر في العهد العثماني والذي كان معمولاً به لدينا (٣)، وقانون النفقة والأحوال الشخصية الذي أصدرته الحكومة المصرية / ١٩٢٠م تحت رقم ٢٥.
(١) لم يأخذ الاجتهادان الحنفي والشافعي بهذا الحكم، بل أوجبا تطبيق قاعدة الاستصحاب العامة في قضية المفقود بالنسبة إلى زوجته وبالنسبة إلى أمواله على السواء. لكن الاجتهاد الشافعي يسوغ لزوجة الغائب طلب التفريق لعدم الإنفاق إذا لم يترك لها مالاً للنفقة. وقاعدة الاستصحاب تقضي بأن الأصل بقاء ما كان على ما كان حتى يقوم الدليل على خلافه. فالمفقود كان حياً، فالأصل استمرار حياته حتى يثبت موته أو حتى تموت أقرانه في بلده فيغلب على الظن عندئذ وفاته. وقبل ذلك لا توزع أمواله بين ورثته، فكذلك تبقى زوجته على عصمته.
ولا يخفى أن هذا الاجتهاد - وإن كان من الوجهة النظرية منسجماً وجارياً على قاعدة قياسية - يؤدي إلى حرج عظيم بالنسبة إلى الزوجة. فإن في الحقوق الزوجية اعتبارات تختلف عن الحقوق المالية. فإذا لم يكن في بقاء المال محتجزاً دهراً طويلاً ضرر أو مفسدة، فإن في بقاء الزوجة بلا زوج ضرراً عظيماً لها لا تقره مقاصد الشريعة، وقد يؤدي إلى مفاسد. فقياس الزوجية على المال قياس مع الفارق. ولمعالجة مثل هذه المشكلات فتح في أصول الشريعة طريق الاستحسان والاستصلاح لاجتناب نتائج غلو القياس، وعلاجاً لمشكلات القواعد النظرية عندما يكون لبعض الحوادث اعتبارات وملابسات خاصة يؤدي معها تطبيق القواعد القياسية إلى محاذير.
Chapter (5) Subsidiary Sources: Istislah. Issue of the missing person: If a husband goes missing in war or similar and his news is unknown, then it becomes permissible for his wife to marry another after the judge decides the dissolution of the marriage and the wife completes her legal waiting period, to prevent harm to the wife from remaining suspended for life, even if the missing husband has sufficient wealth for her maintenance. This is what Umar ibn al-Khattab judged, and the Maliki ijtihad followed Umar's judgment, and likewise the Ottoman Family Law Code, which was in effect in our country, and the Maintenance and Personal Status Law issued by the Egyptian government in 1920 under number 25. (1) The Hanafi and Shafi'i ijtihads did not adopt this ruling; rather they required applying the general rule of istishab in the case of the missing person both for his wife and for his wealth equally. However, the Shafi'i ijtihad allows the wife of an absent husband to seek separation due to non-maintenance if he left no wealth for her. The rule of istishab holds that the original state continues as it was until evidence to the contrary appears. The missing person was alive, so the original state is the continuation of his life until his death is proven or until his peers in his town die, upon which his death becomes probable. Before that, his wealth is not distributed among his heirs, so likewise his wife remains in his bond. It is not hidden that this ijtihad—though theoretically consistent and based on a analogical rule—leads to great hardship for the wife. In marital rights there are considerations different from financial rights. If the continued detainment of wealth for a long time causes no harm or corruption, the wife's remaining without a husband causes great harm that the objectives of Shari'ah do not approve, and may lead to corruption. So comparing marriage to wealth is an analogy with a difference. To address such problems, the path of istihsan and istislah was opened in the principles of Shari'ah to avoid the results of excessive qiyas, and to remedy problems of theoretical rules when certain incidents have special circumstances and contexts, leading to harmful consequences when analogical rules are applied.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage discusses a subsidiary source of law: istislah (public interest). It presents the case of a missing husband (mafqud). The legal problem is that the wife is left in limbo, unable to remarry, while her husband's status is unknown. The Maliki school, following a judgment of Caliph Umar, uses istislah to allow the wife to remarry after a judge dissolves the marriage and she completes her iddah, even if the husband left sufficient wealth. The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools adhere to istishab (presumption of continuity): since the husband was alive, he is presumed still alive, so the marriage remains intact. The author argues that strict qiyas (analogy) between marital rights and financial rights is flawed because the hardship for the wife is far greater. Therefore, istislah is invoked to override the general rule in order to prevent harm and achieve the maqasid of Shari'ah. Note that the Hanafi position is to uphold the general rule unless there is clear evidence of death, but the author, from a legal philosophy perspective, points out the need for flexibility through istihsan/istislah.

Key Terms
المفقود (al-mafqud): A person whose whereabouts and life status are unknown, especially a husband absent for a long time.
الاستصحاب (al-istishab): A legal presumption that a state existing in the past continues until evidence of change.
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): Deriving a ruling based on public interest (maslaha) when no explicit text or qiyas applies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Hanafi
B) Maliki
C) Shafi'i
D) Hanbali

Key Takeaway
Istislah can override strict qiyas when the analogy is flawed and the maqasid demand protection from severe harm.

Real Estate Registration: Balancing Maslaha and Classical Fiqh
Legal provision on registration for real estate contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why modern land registration requirements can be considered valid from a Hanafi perspective, using the principle of maslaha mursalah.

Core Idea
Registration is not required for contract validity in classical fiqh, but can be required for enforcement to prevent fraud.

This passage explores a modern legal requirement that challenges classical Hanafi simplicity. While a sale is valid by offer and acceptance, states impose registration for real rights. The author presents this as an example of regulatory maslaha, which a mufti must understand to navigate between classical fiqh and contemporary regulations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modern rule
Read the passage and note that real estate contracts do not confer real rights unless registered. This is a departure from classical fiqh where offer and acceptance suffice.
Focus on the legal effect: rights in rem vs. personal obligations.
2
Recognize the purpose (maqsad)
The passage states that registration aims to prevent fraud and multiple sales. This is a clear maslaha (public interest) that justifies the rule.
Maslaha mursalah is a recognized source in Hanafi usul for regulatory matters.
3
Compare two legal approaches
The passage contrasts two systems: one where unregistered contracts are void (Jordan), and another where they are binding between parties but not against third parties (Syria). Understand the difference in legal consequences.
From a fiqh perspective, the second approach aligns more with classical Hanafi because validity is preserved, but enforcement is restricted.
4
Connect to Hanafi usul: maslaha mursalah
Explain how the Hanafi school can accept such regulation under siyasa shar'iyya and maslaha, even though there is no explicit textual basis for registration. The rule serves a legitimate public interest without contradicting a definitive text.
This is an example of ta'lil (rational justification) in iftaa.
5
Distinguish between validity and enforceability
Classical Hanafi fiqh considers a sale valid by offer and acceptance (sihha). Modern registration affects enforceability (nafadh) against third parties and may be required for legal effect. This distinction is crucial for a mufti.
The contract remains valid between the parties even without registration, but rights in rem are not transferred.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

30 - The legal provisions that today require that real estate contracts do not confer real rights in the property unless registered in the land registry, for numerous regulatory and reformative purposes, including preventing the owner from fraud and deception on people, such as selling the same property to several persons successively, taking all prices or debts without the later one knowing of the earlier contract (1). (1) Today's land laws in different countries differ in their stance towards real estate contracts if they are made in ordinary way between the contracting parties without registration in the land registry. Some countries consider these contracts void ab initio, creating no effect between the parties themselves nor vis-à-vis others, such that if one party refuses to register, the other cannot force him through court due to the contract's nullity. The Jordanian law follows this. Other countries consider the unregistered contract as concluded and binding between them, but it does not produce effects vis-à-vis others unless registered. If one refuses to register, the other has the right to sue and obtain a judgment for registration. For example, if it is a sale, before registration the owner vis-à-vis the tenant remains the seller, so the tenant pays rent to the seller, not the buyer. This is what our new civil law follows.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage discusses a modern legal provision requiring registration of real estate contracts for the transfer of property rights. Although this is a civil law (qanun), it is based on the principle of maslaha (public interest) to prevent multiple sales and fraud. The author presents this as an example of how public interest can shape legal rules. The passage compares different legal systems: some consider unregistered contracts void (like Jordan), others consider them binding between parties but not against third parties (like the Syrian new civil code). From a Hanafi perspective, fiqh traditionally does not require registration for the validity of a sale; a sale is valid by offer and acceptance. However, modern regulations can impose additional requirements for the enforcement of rights (especially against third parties) to protect public order and prevent fraud. This is an example of a regulatory rule (siyasa shari'ah).

Key Terms
السجل العقاري (al-sijil al-'aqari): Official land registry where property rights are recorded.
حق عيني (haqq 'ayni): Real right (right in rem) that is effective against the world, as opposed to personal rights.
مصلحة مرسلة (maslaha mursala): Consideration of public interest not explicitly mentioned in the primary sources, used as a basis for legislation in matters of worldly benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Modern registration requirements for real estate are not a departure from Hanafi principles but a legitimate regulatory measure based on maslaha to prevent fraud, separating contract validity from enforceability of real rights.
