Istislah and Istihsan: Overlap with Modern Examples
Explaining that many modern legal reforms are based on maslaha mursala and also constitute istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify rulings based on maslaha mursala and explain how they simultaneously constitute istihsan by departing from qiyas.

Core Idea
Istislah and istihsan often overlap: a ruling serving public interest may also be a departure from strict analogy.

In this passage, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa presents modern examples—such as requiring registration for real estate contracts and prohibiting out-of-court acknowledgements without written evidence—to demonstrate how many reforms in private law are grounded in maslaha mursala (unrestricted interest). He then notes that these very same rulings also qualify as istihsan (juristic preference) because they deviate from what strict qiyas would require. Understanding this overlap is crucial for a mufti evaluating contemporary legal reforms through the lens of Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modern examples
List the three legal reforms given: debtor transactions, land registration, and written evidence for acknowledgements.
These are all in the realm of private rights (huquq khasah).
2
Explain the basis in maslaha mursala for each
For each example, identify the unmixed interest (maslaha) it serves: protecting creditors, preventing fraud, avoiding forgery.
No specific textual evidence is cited; the interest is the sole basis.
3
Contrast each with the qiyas rule that would otherwise apply
State what strict qiyas would require: freedom of debtor to dispose, validity of oral contracts, admissibility of witness evidence.
These counterfactuals are explicitly mentioned in the text.
4
Conclude that each example is simultaneously istislah and istihsan
Show that the ruling is istislah because it serves a maslaha, and istihsan because it departs from qiyas for a stronger reason.
The author emphasizes the overlap explicitly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني : مصادر الفقه الإسلامي
الذي أصدرته الحكومة المصرية / ۱۹۲۰م تحت رقم / ٢٥ .
۲۰ - فتوى المتأخرين من فقهاء المذهب الحنبلي، ثم الحنفي بعدم نفاذ التصرفات المالية الصادرة من المدين بدين مستغرق ولو كان غير محجور عليه قضائياً إلا فيما يزيد من أمواله عن وفاء الدين، وذلك كيلا يلجأ بهذه التصرفات من هبة ووقف ونحوهما إلى تهريب أمواله من وجه الدائنين، فإذا تصرف كان تصرفه موقوفاً على إجازة غرمائه الدائنين كما سيأتي إيضاحه في محله (ر: ف ۵/۳۸ و ۲/۷۷).
٣٠ - الأحكام القانونية التي تقضي اليوم بأنَّ العقود العقارية لا تكسب المتعاقد حقوقاً عينية في العقار ما لم تسجل في السجل العقاري، وذلك لمقاصد تنظيمية وإصلاحية عديدة منها منع صاحب العقار من التلاعب والاحتيال على الناس بأن عقاره مثلاً يبيع من عدة أشخاص على التعاقب جميعاً أثماناً أو ديوناً دون أن يدري اللاحق أو يرهنه لديهم ويأخذ منهم بعقد السابق (۱).
٤ - ومن هذا القبيل أيضاً المنع القانوني من إثبات الإقرار الذي يدعى وقوعه خارج مجلس القضاء إلا ببينة خطيَّة، فلا يقبل إثباته بالبينة الشخصية أي شهادة شهود وذلك لكثرة حوادث تزوير الشهادة على الإقرار وسهولة هذا التزوير فتموت به الحقوق وترد دعاوي أصحابها . فكل من ثبت عليه حق فإنه لا يقبل منه أن يثبت إقرار صاحبه بخلافه خارج مجلس القضاة إلا بوثيقة مكتوبة. وهذا المنع القانوني منذ العهد العثماني في هذه البلاد (۱)
١٣/٥ - كل ذلك إنما هو من قبيل بناء الأحكام على مقتضى المصالح المرسلة في باب الحقوق الخاصة
ويلاحظ هنا أن معظم أمثلة هذا النوع تعد أيضاً من قبيل الاستحسان لأنها تقوم على أساس الترخص في مخالفة قواعد قياسية كانت تقتضي خلاف هذه الأحكام.
فقد كان مقتضى القواعد القياسية صحة العقود العقارية وترتب آثارها عليها ولو عُقدت سراً بين العاقدين أسوة للعقار بغيره من الأموال؛ وكذا بقاء زوجة المفقود حتى تثبت وفاته أو يموت أقرانه ؛ وسماع الدعوى بالحق مهما تقادم .
فتلك الأحكام والتدابير الاستثنائية التي قررت في هذه الأمثلة وأمثالها استصلاح بالنظر إلى المصلحة التي أوجبتها ؛ وهي في الوقت نفسه استحسان بالنظر إلى أنها قد خولفت فيها القواعد القياسية، على ما سبق بيانه من الوفق والفرق بين الاستحسان والاستصلاح.
(1) يلحظ في هذا المقام أن قانون البينات الجديد الصادر في سورية سنة ١٣٦٦ ١٩٤٧ م أصبح يسوع إثبات الإقرار الواقع خارج مجلس القضاء بالشهادة الالتزامات غير التعاقدية مطلقاً وفي التعاقدية التي لا تتجاوز قيمتها مائة ليرة سورية، ووفقاً لقواعد الإثبات العامة المقررة في القانون كما يظهر من المواد / ٥٢ - ٥٨ و ٩٥ و ١٠٢ من القانون المذكور.
Chapter Two: Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence
The one issued by the Egyptian government in 1920 under number 25.
20 - The fatwa of later Hanbali jurists, then Hanafi, regarding the non-enforceability of financial transactions made by a debtor whose debt is all-encompassing, even if he is not legally interdicted, except for what exceeds his debt, so that he does not resort to such transactions (gift, endowment, etc.) to smuggle his assets from creditors. If he transacts, his transaction is suspended pending the approval of his creditors, as will be explained in its place (ref: Vol. 5 p. 38 and Vol. 2 p. 77).
30 - Legal rulings today that real estate contracts do not confer real rights in the property unless registered in the land registry, for numerous regulatory and reform purposes, including preventing the property owner from manipulating and defrauding people by, for example, selling his property to several persons successively, or mortgaging it to them and taking [money] from them with a prior contract (1).
4 - Of this kind also is the legal prohibition of proving an acknowledgement claimed to have occurred outside the courtroom except by written evidence; thus it cannot be proven by personal testimony (witnesses) due to the frequency of forgery of testimony on acknowledgement and the ease of such forgery, causing rights to die and their owners' claims to be rejected. So whoever has a right established against him, he is not allowed to prove his opponent's acknowledgement to the contrary outside the courtroom except by a written document. This legal prohibition has been in place since the Ottoman era in these lands (1).
13/5 - All of this is of the type of building rulings on the basis of unmixed interests (masalih mursala) in the realm of private rights.
It is noted here that most examples of this type are also considered istihsan because they are based on a concession in violating qiyas rules that would have required the opposite of these rulings.
For the qiyas rules would have required the validity of real estate contracts and the attachment of their effects to them even if secretly concluded between the parties, like other properties; also the persistence of the wife of a missing person until his death is proven or his contemporaries die; and the hearing of claims regardless of how long they have been outstanding.
Those exceptional rulings and measures established in these examples and their like are istislah from the perspective of the interest that necessitated them; and they are at the same time istihsan from the perspective that the qiyas rules have been violated in them, according to what has been previously explained of the agreement and difference between istihsan and istislah.
(1) It is observed here that the new Evidence Law issued in Syria in 1366 AH / 1947 CE allows the proof of acknowledgement occurring outside the courtroom by testimony for non-contractual obligations absolutely, and for contractual ones whose value does not exceed one hundred Syrian liras, according to the general rules of evidence established in the law, as appears from Articles 52-58 and 95 and 102 of the said law.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm begins by listing examples of modern legal rulings that are based on the concept of public interest (maslaha mursala). The first example is the fatwa of later Hanbali and Hanafi jurists that a debtor whose debts exceed his assets cannot dispose of his property in a way that harms creditors, and such transactions are suspended subject to creditor approval. The second example is the requirement of registration for real estate contracts to confer real rights, preventing fraud. The third is the prohibition of proving out-of-court acknowledgements by witnesses alone, requiring written evidence to prevent forgery. After these examples, the author states that all such rulings are built on maslaha mursala in the field of private rights. He then notes that most of these examples also qualify as istihsan (juristic preference) because they involve a departure from analogical reasoning (qiyas). Under strict qiyas, real estate contracts would be valid without registration, the wife of a missing person would remain indefinitely waiting, and old claims would always be heard. However, due to practical considerations, these rulings deviate from qiyas. Thus, these rulings are simultaneously istislah (based on maslaha) and istihsan (departure from qiyas for a stronger reason). The footnote adds that Syrian law later allowed some oral proof of acknowledgements within limits. The passage highlights the interplay between these two secondary sources of law.

Key Terms
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): بناء الأحكام على مقتضى المصالح المرسلة في باب الحقوق الخاصة
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): الترخص في مخالفة قواعد قياسية لمصلحة أقوى
المصالح المرسلة (al-masalih al-mursala): مصالح لا دليل على اعتبارها أو إلغائها من الشارع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the relationship between most examples of istislah (maslaha mursala) and istihsan?
A) They are completely distinct and never overlap.
B) Istislah is a subset of istihsan.
C) Most examples of istislah also qualify as istihsan because they deviate from qiyas.
D) Istihsan is only used in criminal law, while istislah is for civil law.

Key Takeaway
Many modern legal reforms are simultaneously istislah and istihsan: they serve public interest while departing from strict qiyas.

The Overlap of Istislah and Istihsan: Three Concrete Cases
Explaining the overlap between istislah and istihsan through three examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the concepts of istislah and istihsan to new cases by identifying when a ruling that contravenes qiyas can be justified by public interest.

Core Idea
Istislah and istihsan overlap whenever a public-interest ruling contravenes qiyas — they are two faces of the same exception.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa clarifies the relationship between istislah (consideration of unrestricted interests) and istihsan (juristic preference) by examining three classic examples. He shows that many rulings we might call istislah are also istihsan because they involve departing from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more beneficial outcome. This overlap is crucial for understanding Hanafi legal methodology, especially when dealing with private rights and modern legislation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three illustrative cases
Locate the three examples mentioned: secret real estate contracts, the wife of a missing person, and hearing a lawsuit after long prescription. Write them down.
These are the core examples that demonstrate the overlap.
2
State the qiyas rule for each case
For each example, determine the rule that strict analogy would produce. For contracts: validity even if secret; for missing spouse: marriage continues until death proven; for lawsuits: hearing regardless of time.
These qiyas rules are the 'default' positions that are being set aside.
3
State the exceptional ruling based on public interest
For each example, state the actual ruling: secret contracts are invalid (or require registration); missing spouse allows remarriage after a period; lawsuits are time-barred. Note that these serve a public interest.
These are istislah because they aim to achieve maslaha.
4
Recognize the double classification
Understand that each exceptional ruling is simultaneously istislah (based on maslaha) and istihsan (because it contravenes a qiyas rule). The passage explicitly says so.
This is the key insight: istislah and istihsan are not mutually exclusive.
5
Examine the modern footnote
Read the footnote about the Syrian Evidence Law (1366 AH/1947 CE) allowing verbal acknowledgments to be proven by testimony. This is another example of istislah/istihsan in modern legislation.
Shows how classical principles apply to contemporary legal systems.

Source Text

ويلاحظ هنا أن معظم أمثلة هذا النوع تعد أيضاً من قبيل الاستحسان لأنها تقوم على أساس الترخص في مخالفة قواعد قياسية كانت تقتضي خلاف هذه الأحكام.
فقد كان مقتضى القواعد القياسية صحة العقود العقارية وترتب آثارها عليها ولو عُقدت سراً بين العاقدين أسوة للعقار بغيره من الأموال؛ وكذا بقاء زوجة المفقود حتى تثبت وفاته أو يموت أقرانه ؛ وسماع الدعوى بالحق مهما تقادم .
فتلك الأحكام والتدابير الاستثنائية التي قررت في هذه الأمثلة وأمثالها استصلاح بالنظر إلى المصلحة التي أوجبتها؛ وهي في الوقت نفسه استحسان بالنظر إلى أنها قد خولفت فيها القواعد القياسية، على ما سبق بيانه من الوفق والفرق بين الاستحسان والاستصلاح.
(1) يلحظ في هذا المقام أن قانون البينات الجديد الصادر في سورية سنة ١٣٦٦ ١٩٤٧ م أصبح يسوع إثبات الإقرار الواقع خارج مجلس القضاء بالشهادة الالتزامات غير التعاقدية مطلقاً وفي التعاقدية التي لا تتجاوز قيمتها مائة ليرة سورية، ووفقاً لقواعد الإثبات العامة المقررة في القانون كما يظهر من المواد / ٥٢ - ٥٨ و ٩٥ و ١٠٢ من القانون المذكور.
All of that is only from the category of building rulings on the basis of masalih mursalah (unrestricted interests) in the chapter of private rights. It is noted here that most examples of this type are also considered from the category of istihsan because they are based on leniency in contradicting qiyas rules that would have necessitated contrary rulings. For the qiyas rules required the validity of real estate contracts and the attachment of their effects to them even if they were made secretly between the contractors, like real estate with other properties; likewise the persistence of the wife of a missing person until his death is proven or his peers die; and hearing a lawsuit regardless of how long it has been overdue. So those exceptional rulings and measures decided in these examples and the like are istislah in view of the interest that necessitated them; and they are at the same time istihsan in view that the qiyas rules have been contravened in them, according to the previously explained agreement and difference between istihsan and istislah. (1) It is observed here that the new Evidence Law issued in Syria in 1366 AH/1947 CE allowed the proof of an acknowledgment made outside the courtroom by testimony for non-contractual obligations absolutely, and for contractual obligations not exceeding one hundred Syrian liras, according to the general rules of proof established in the law, as appears from Articles 52-58, 95, and 102 of the said law.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between istislah (consideration of public interest) and istihsan (juristic preference) in Hanafi fiqh. The author clarifies that many rulings derived through istislah also fall under istihsan because they involve departing from strict analogy (qiyas). Examples include: upholding secret real estate contracts (contrary to the qiyas that would require publicity), not dissolving the marriage of a missing person immediately (contrary to the qiyas of divorce), and allowing lawsuits even after long prescription (contrary to the qiyas of limitation). The footnote mentions a Syrian law that allows proving verbal acknowledgments by testimony, which is another departure from the general qiyas of evidence. Thus, the passage emphasizes that istislah and istihsan are interrelated: every istislah is also istihsan when it contravenes qiyas.

Key Terms
استصلاح (istislah): Building rulings on the basis of unrestricted public interests (masalih mursalah) when no clear text or consensus exists
استحسان (istihsan): Departing from a qiyas rule in favor of a different ruling due to a stronger evidence or necessity
قواعد قياسية (qawa'id qiyasiyyah): Rules derived through strict analogical reasoning (qiyas) that would normally apply to similar cases

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are the examples of secret contracts, missing spouse, and prescription considered both istislah and istihsan?
A) Because they are based on a revelatory text
B) Because they involve departing from qiyas rules for a public interest
C) Because they are purely speculative
D) Because they follow the literal meaning of the Quran

Key Takeaway
Istislah and istihsan are not mutually exclusive; in many cases, a single legal ruling is both istislah (grounded in public interest) and istihsan (a departure from analogy).

Hanafi and Maliki Views on Istihsan and Istislah
Comparing Hanafi and Maliki approaches to istihsan and istislah as legal sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi and Maliki positions on istihsan and istislah and explain the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence of the difference).

Core Idea
Hanafis treat istihsan as a branch of qiyas, not independent; Malikis give masalih mursalah broader scope.

This passage examines how two major schools, the Hanafi and Maliki, conceptualize istihsan and istislah. Understanding their differing frameworks is essential for a mufti when weighing public interest against strict analogy. The Hanafi school systematized istihsan into a binary classification, keeping it tied to qiyas, while the Maliki school elevated masalih mursalah to a more fundamental source, subsuming istihsan under it. This difference reflects deeper methodological commitments that affect daily fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi classification of istihsan
Recognize that the Hanafi school divides istihsan into two types: qiyasi istihsan (which is essentially qiyas) and daruri istihsan (necessity-based). Only daruri istihsan is comparable to istislah.
Qiyasi istihsan is not a departure from qiyas but a preference for a stronger analogy.
2
Understand the basis of daruri istihsan
Daruri istihsan is grounded in the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj) and the general objectives of the sharia (maqasid). It modifies qiyas when qiyas leads to difficulty.
The passage links this to the early term 'al-ra'y' and later istislah.
3
Contrast the Maliki approach
The Maliki school presented masalih mursalah (unrestricted public interest) in a broader form, making istihsan a specific branch of it, used when departing from qiyas.
For Malikis, maslaha can be an independent source; for Hanafis, it operates within the qiyas framework.
4
Analyze the Hanafi refusal to count istihsan as an independent source
The passage states that Hanafi jurists do not consider istihsan an additional source beyond the four primary sources; it is a branch of qiyas used to remedy its flaws.
This methodological conservatism keeps the system closed but flexible through necessity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafi muftis will only depart from qiyas when there is clear necessity (darurah) or a stronger analogy; Maliki muftis may give wider scope to public interest (maslaha) even without acute necessity.
This affects rulings in areas like financial contracts and public welfare.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٤/٥ - الاستحسان والاستصلاح في المذاهب الاجتهادية : اختلفت الأنظار الاجتهادية في اعتبار الاستحسان والاستصلاح:
فقد فتح الاجتهاد الحنفي طريقة الاستحسان وسماها بهذا الاسم وأسسها على نظام مضبوط عندما لمس الحاجة إلى معالجة غلو القياس ومشكلاته بنظر مستمد من أساليب الشريعة نفسها في علاج المساوىء والطوارىء ومن مقاصدها العامة في العدل والإصلاح.
وقد رأينا في بحث الاستحسان أنَّ الاجتهاد الحنفي يقسمه إلى :نوعين الاستحسان القياسي واستحسان الضرورة فالاستحسان القياسي ليس مقصوداً هنا في هذه الموازنة لأنه ضرب من القياس كما تقدم، وإنما المقصود هنا هو استحسان الضرورة لأنه هو شقيق الاستصلاح.
وقد رأينا أيضاً هناك أنَّ استحسان الضرورة عند الحنفية يستند إلى رعاية المصلحة تعديلاً لطريق القياس عندما يؤدي القياس إلى إحراج، لأن من قواعد الشريعة رفع الحرج فهو، أي الاستحسان عند الحنفية، التفات إلى مقاصد الشريعة العامة في ابتغاء الأصلح، وكان يسمى هذا الالتفات باسم «الرأي» في العصر الأول، ثم سمي باسم الاستصلاح فيما بعد، أو المصالح المرسلة.
والظاهر من نظرية الاجتهاد الحنفي في الاستحسان ومن عبارات فقهائه أنهم لا يعدونه مصدراً مستقلاً زائداً عن المصادر الأربعة الأساسية . وإنما يرونه طريقاً متفرعاً من القياس المشروع باتجاه معاكس له يسلك لمعالجة ما يظهر من مساوىء القياس، بناء على قاعدة الضرورة ورفع الحرج.
١٥/٥ - ثم جاء الاجتهاد المالكي فأبرز نظرية المصالح المرسلة في صورة أعم بحيث جعل الاستحسان فرعاً منها مخصوصاً بحال مخالفة
14/5 - Istihsan and Istislah in the Independent Schools of Jurisprudence: The independent legal viewpoints differed regarding the consideration of istihsan and istislah. The Hanafi school opened the method of istihsan, named it by this name, and founded it upon a regulated system when it felt the need to address the extremism of qiyas and its problems with a view derived from the methods of the sharia itself in addressing defects and emergencies and from its general objectives of justice and reform. We have seen in the discussion of istihsan that Hanafi ijtihad divides it into two types: qiyasi istihsan and daruri istihsan. Qiyasi istihsan is not intended here in this comparison because it is a type of qiyas as previously mentioned; rather, what is intended here is daruri istihsan because it is the sibling of istislah. We also saw there that daruri istihsan among the Hanafis is based on consideration of interest as a modification of the method of qiyas when qiyas leads to difficulty, because from the principles of the sharia is removing hardship. So it (i.e., istihsan among the Hanafis) is an attention to the general objectives of the sharia in seeking what is most beneficial. This attention was called 'al-ra'y' in the first era, then later called istislah, or masalih mursalah. It appears from the Hanafi theory of ijtihad in istihsan and from the statements of its jurists that they do not consider it an independent source additional to the four primary sources. Rather, they see it as a path branching from legitimate qiyas in an opposite direction, taken to address the flaws that appear in qiyas, based on the principle of necessity and removing hardship. 15/5 - Then the Maliki school came and brought forth the theory of masalih mursalah in a more general form, making istihsan a branch of it, specific to cases of contravention.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage compares how different schools treat istihsan and istislah. The Hanafi school systematized istihsan, dividing it into two types: 'qiyasi istihsan' (which is essentially qiyas) and 'daruri istihsan' (necessity-based istihsan). The latter is the focus because it is closely related to istislah: both involve considering public interest to depart from strict analogy when it leads to hardship. The Hanafis ground this in the general principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj). Importantly, they do not regard istihsan as an independent source of law; it is merely a branch of qiyas used to remedy its defects. In contrast, the Maliki school presented the concept of masalih mursalah in a broader form, making istihsan a subcategory of it. This indicates that for Malikis, the consideration of unrestricted public interest is a more fundamental source, whereas Hanafis keep istihsan tied to qiyas and necessity.

Key Terms
استحسان الضرورة (istihsan al-darurah): Necessity-based istihsan: departing from qiyas due to hardship or necessity, closely related to istislah
مقاصد الشريعة العامة (maqasid al-shari'ah al-'ammah): General objectives of Islamic law, such as justice and reform, which istihsan seeks to uphold
رفع الحرج (raf' al-haraj): The principle of removing hardship, a foundational qawida that justifies daruri istihsan

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How do the Hanafis classify istihsan relative to qiyas?
A) Istihsan is an independent source equal to the Quran
B) Istihsan is a branch of qiyas used to remedy its defects
C) Istihsan is the same as consensus
D) Istihsan is a type of custom

Key Takeaway
Hanafis do not consider istihsan an independent source but a branch of qiyas based on necessity and removal of hardship.

Istihsan and Istislah: Hanafi, Maliki, and Shafi'i Perspectives
Comparative analysis of istihsan and istislah across three madhhabs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi view of istihsan as a regulated branch of qiyas, contrast it with the Maliki theory of masalih mursala, and evaluate the Shafi'i rejection of both.

Core Idea
Istihsan and istislah are disciplined tools, not arbitrary preferences — each school regulates them differently.

This lesson explores how the Hanafi, Maliki, and Shafi'i schools approach istihsan (juristic preference) and istislah (public interest). The Hanafis developed istihsan as a necessity-driven modification of qiyas; the Malikis elevated masalih mursala to an independent source; the Shafi'is rejected both as lacking objective criteria. Understanding these differences is crucial for fatwa methodology across the schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi framework for istihsan
State the two types of istihsan (qiyasi and daruri) and explain why daruri istihsan is the focus here. Note that it is based on the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj).
The Hanafis do not consider istihsan an independent source; it is a branch of qiyas.
2
Contrast with the Maliki theory of masalih mursala
Explain that the Malikis treat masalih mursala as an independent source and view istihsan as a subset of it. Note the historical development: the Maliki formulation came later and is more systematic.
Both schools share the same core idea of public interest, but differ on classification.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i rejection
State the Shafi'i argument: Sharia covers all rulings via text, indication, or qiyas; istihsan lacks controls and leads to arbitrary decisions.
Imam al-Shafi'i wrote a dedicated critique in al-Umm.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequences)
Identify a concrete example like the salam sale (advance payment) where Hanafi istihsan allows it despite qiyas prohibition, while Shafi'i qiyas would forbid it.
This shows the real-world impact of the methodological difference.

الباب الثاني : مصادر الفقه الإسلامي
١٤/٥ - الاستحسان والاستصلاح في المذاهب الاجتهادية : اختلفت الأنظار الاجتهادية في اعتبار الاستحسان والاستصلاح:
فقد فتح الاجتهاد الحنفي طريقة الاستحسان وسماها بهذا الاسم وأسسها على نظام مضبوط عندما لمس الحاجة إلى معالجة غلو القياس ومشكلاته بنظر مستمد من أساليب الشريعة نفسها في علاج المساوىء والطوارىء ومن مقاصدها العامة في العدل والإصلاح.
وقد رأينا في بحث الاستحسان أنَّ الاجتهاد الحنفي يقسمه إلى :نوعين الاستحسان القياسي واستحسان الضرورة فالاستحسان القياسي ليس مقصوداً هنا في هذه الموازنة لأنه ضرب من القياس كما تقدم، وإنما المقصود هنا هو استحسان الضرورة لأنه هو شقيق الاستصلاح.
وقد رأينا أيضاً هناك أنَّ استحسان الضرورة عند الحنفية يستند إلى رعاية المصلحة تعديلاً لطريق القياس عندما يؤدي القياس إلى إحراج، لأن من قواعد الشريعة رفع الحرج فهو، أي الاستحسان عند الحنفية، التفات إلى مقاصد الشريعة العامة في ابتغاء الأصلح، وكان يسمى هذا الالتفات باسم «الرأي» في العصر الأول، ثم سمي باسم الاستصلاح فيما بعد، أو المصالح المرسلة.
والظاهر من نظرية الاجتهاد الحنفي في الاستحسان ومن عبارات فقهائه أنهم لا يعدونه مصدراً مستقلاً زائداً عن المصادر الأربعة الأساسية . وإنما يرونه طريقاً متفرعاً من القياس المشروع باتجاه معاكس له يسلك لمعالجة ما يظهر من مساوىء القياس، بناء على قاعدة الضرورة ورفع الحرج.
١٥/٥ - ثم جاء الاجتهاد المالكي فأبرز نظرية المصالح المرسلة في صورة أعم بحيث جعل الاستحسان فرعاً منها مخصوصاً بحال مخالفة
القواعد القياسية عندما تقضي المصلحة بمخالفة تلك القواعد اجتناباً لمشكلة يؤدي إليها القياس كما تقدم بينما كانت فكرة المصالح في طريقة الاجتهاد الحنفي تعد هي فرعاً من الاستحسان، لأن الحنفية اتخذوا من استحسان الضرورة الذي يخالف فيه مقتضى القواعد القياسية نقطة انطلاق تؤدي حتماً إلى اعتماد المصالح المرسلة التي لا مخالفة فيها لشيء من القواعد . والمعروف عن الاجتهاد المالكي أنه يعتبر المصالح المرسلة مصدراً مستقلاً دلت على اعتباره نصوص الشريعة كما دلت على القياس، فتبنى على أساسها الأحكام الشرعية عند فقدان النص التشريعي في الحادثة أو فيما يشابهها فتكون المصالح هي الدليل عندما لا يكون دليل سواها، كما يخالف بها القياس المستند إلى نص في الحوادث المشابهة عندما يؤدي اطراد القياس إلى خلاف المصلحة. وهذه الحالة الأخيرة يبرز فيها النظر إلى المصالح المرسلة في صورة الاستحسان المخالف لمقتضى القياس (ر: ف ٩/٥)
ومن هنا يلوح لنا وجه الاتفاق بين المذهبين الحنفي والمالكي في نظرية الاستصلاح فالفكرة فيهما ،واحدة ولكن المذهب المالكي لتأخره عن الحنفي في التاريخ تركزت فيه الصياغة الفنية الفقهية لقاعدة المصالح المرسلة وشرائطها فبرزت فيه واشتهر بها.
١٦/٥ - ثم ظهر الاجتهاد الشافعي بإنكار نظريتي الاستحسان والمصالح المرسلة بحجة أن الشريعة الإسلامية قد تكفلت ببيان كل ما يحتاج الإنسان إلى معرفته من الأحكام إما بالنص الصريح، أو بالإشارة، أو بطريق القياس المشروع، وأنَّ الاستحسان لا ضابط له ولا مقاييس يقاس بها الحق بالباطل، فلو جاز لكل مفت أو حاكم أو مجتهد أن يستحسن لأصبح الأمر فُرُطاً .

Chapter Two: Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence
14/5 - Istihsan and Istislah in the Ijtihad Schools: The ijtihad perspectives differed regarding the consideration of istihsan and istislah:
The Hanafi ijtihad opened the method of istihsan and named it with this name and founded it on a regulated system when it perceived the need to address the exaggeration of qiyas and its problems with a perspective derived from the methods of the Sharia itself in dealing with evils and emergencies and from its general objectives of justice and reform.
We have seen in the discussion of istihsan that the Hanafi ijtihad divides it into two types: qiyasi istihsan and daruri istihsan. Qiyasi istihsan is not intended here in this comparison because it is a type of qiyas as previously mentioned. Rather, what is intended here is daruri istihsan because it is the twin of istislah.
We also saw there that daruri istihsan according to the Hanafis is based on observing maslaha as a modification of the method of qiyas when qiyas leads to hardship. Because one of the rules of Sharia is the removal of hardship. So istihsan according to the Hanafis is an attention to the general objectives of Sharia in seeking what is more beneficial. This attention was called 'al-ray' in the first era, then later called istislah, or masalih mursala.
It is apparent from the Hanafi ijtihad theory of istihsan and from the statements of its jurists that they do not consider it an independent source additional to the four basic sources. Rather, they see it as a path branching from the legislated qiyas in an opposite direction, taken to address the evils of qiyas that appear, based on the principle of necessity and removal of hardship.
15/5 - Then came the Maliki ijtihad and brought forth the theory of masalih mursala in a more general form, making istihsan a branch of it specific to the case of violating
qiyas rules when maslaha requires violating those rules to avoid a problem to which qiyas leads, as previously mentioned. While the concept of maslaha in the Hanafi ijtihad method was considered a branch of istihsan, because the Hanafis took daruri istihsan, which violates the requirements of qiyas rules, as a starting point that inevitably leads to the adoption of masalih mursala that contain no violation of any rules. It is known about Maliki ijtihad that it considers masalih mursala an independent source whose consideration is indicated by the texts of Sharia just as they indicate qiyas. Thus legal rulings are established based on it when there is no legislative text on the incident or on what resembles it. So maslaha becomes the evidence when there is no evidence other than it. And they may violate with it qiyas based on text in similar incidents when the application of qiyas leads to contradiction of maslaha. This last case highlights the view of masalih mursala in the form of istihsan that violates the requirement of qiyas (see: 9/5).
Hence the point of agreement between the Hanafi and Maliki schools in the theory of istislah becomes clear: the idea in both is one, but the Maliki school, being later in history, had the technical juristic formulation of the principle of masalih mursala and its conditions concentrated in it, so it emerged and became famous for it.
16/5 - Then the Shafi'i ijtihad appeared, denying the theories of istihsan and masalih mursala on the grounds that Islamic Sharia has undertaken to explain everything a person needs to know of rulings, either by explicit text, or by indication, or by the method of legislated qiyas; and that istihsan has no control and no measures by which truth can be weighed against falsehood; if every mufti, judge, or mujtahid were allowed to perform istihsan, the matter would become excessive.
Imam al-Shafi'i in his book 'al-Umm' dedicated a special section called 'Kitab'.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage compares the Hanafi, Maliki, and Shafi'i schools on the concepts of istihsan (juristic preference) and istislah (public interest/masalih mursala) as subsidiary sources of Islamic law. The author begins by stating that ijtihad perspectives differed on these sources. For the Hanafi school, istihsan is a regulated method developed to address the rigidity of qiyas (analogy) when it leads to hardship. The Hanafis divide istihsan into two types: qiyasi (analogical) istihsan, which is essentially a form of qiyas, and daruri (necessity) istihsan, which is the focus here because it is akin to istislah. Daruri istihsan is based on the principle of removing hardship and serves as a modification of qiyas by considering the general objectives of Sharia (maqasid). The Hanafis do not consider istihsan an independent source; rather it is a branch of qiyas applied in an opposite direction. The Maliki school, meanwhile, presents masalih mursala as a more general theory, with istihsan as a subcategory specifically for cases where maslaha requires departing from qiyas rules. In contrast, the Hanafis view masalih mursala as a derivative of istihsan. The Malikis regard masalih mursala as an independent source, supported by Sharia texts just like qiyas, used when no specific text exists, and even to override analogical deduction that contradicts maslaha. The passage notes that despite these differences, both schools share the same core idea of considering public interest. The Shafi'i school, however, rejects both istihsan and masalih mursala, arguing that Sharia has provided for all rulings through explicit text, indication, or valid analogy. Shafi'i criticized istihsan as lacking objective criteria, potentially leading to arbitrary rulings. Imam al-Shafi'i wrote a special treatise on this in his book 'al-Umm'.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A method of juristic preference where a ruling is adopted based on considerations of necessity or public interest, departing from strict qiyas. The Hanafis divide it into qiyasi (analogical) and daruri (necessity) istihsan.
الاستصلاح / المصالح المرسلة (al-istislah / al-masalih al-mursala): Public interest not explicitly regulated by a text or consensus, used as a basis for legal rulings. The Maliki school considers it an independent source; the Hanafi school sees it as a branch of istihsan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the basis for daruri istihsan?
A) The principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj)
B) The principle of istislah as an independent source
C) The rejection of qiyas in all cases
D) The consensus of the companions

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses daruri istihsan as a regulated, necessity-driven modification of qiyas, distinct from both the Maliki independent masalih mursala and the Shafi'i rejection.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

15 min
Istihsan and Masalih Mursalah: Hanafi, Maliki, and Shafi'i Approaches
Comparing Hanafi, Maliki, and Shafi'i approaches to masalih mursalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Hanafi, Maliki, and Shafi'i positions on masalih mursalah and istihsan, and evaluate the Shafi'i critique in light of Hanafi-Maliki practice.

Core Idea
Masalih mursalah is either subsumed under istihsan (Hanafi) or an independent source (Maliki); Shafi'is reject both, but even al-Ghazali conceded necessity-based exceptions.

This lesson examines the three madhahib's treatment of public interest (maslahah) as a legal source. While Hanafis treat it as a branch of istihsan and Malikis as an independent principle, Shafi'is reject it as a basis for legislation. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, defends the Hanafi-Maliki approach, showing that it is constrained by Shari'ah objectives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
State the Hanafi view (masalih as branch of istihsan), the Maliki view (independent source), and the Shafi'i view (rejection).
Note the subtle difference: Hanafis arrive at maslahah via necessity-based istihsan; Malikis use it directly.
2
Analyze Hanafi-Maliki common ground
Recognize that both schools accept the consideration of public interest when no explicit text governs, and both allow overriding qiyas that would lead to harm.
The author notes Maliki formulation came later and became more systematic.
3
Understand the Shafi'i rejection
Examine al-Shafi'i's argument: Shari'ah is complete; istihsan lacks objective criteria and leads to arbitrary legislation.
Al-Ghazali later softened this, accepting qiyasi istihsan and darurah cases but reclassifying them as qiyas.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi-Maliki response
Consider the author's critique that Shafi'i rejection is exaggerated; istihsan is guided by Shari'ah objectives and evidences.
The safeguards for masalih mursalah (compatibility with Shari'ah objectives) prevent arbitrariness.
5
Apply the safeguards in practice
Apply the condition that a maslahah must be compatible with the universal objectives of Shari'ah and not contradict any definitive proof.
This condition is derived from the Maliki practice as quoted by Abu Zahra from al-Shatibi.

الفصل (٥) المصادر التبعية الاستصلاح
القواعد القياسية عندما تقضي المصلحة بمخالفة تلك القواعد اجتناباً لمشكلة يؤدي إليها القياس كما تقدم بينما كانت فكرة المصالح في طريقة الاجتهاد الحنفي تعد هي فرعاً من الاستحسان، لأن الحنفية اتخذوا من استحسان الضرورة الذي يخالف فيه مقتضى القواعد القياسية نقطة انطلاق تؤدي حتماً إلى اعتماد المصالح المرسلة التي لا مخالفة فيها لشيء من القواعد . والمعروف عن الاجتهاد المالكي أنه يعتبر المصالح المرسلة مصدراً مستقلاً دلت على اعتباره نصوص الشريعة كما دلت على القياس، فتبنى على أساسها الأحكام الشرعية عند فقدان النص التشريعي في الحادثة أو فيما يشابهها فتكون المصالح هي الدليل عندما لا يكون دليل سواها، كما يخالف بها القياس المستند إلى نص في الحوادث المشابهة عندما يؤدي اطراد القياس إلى خلاف المصلحة. وهذه الحالة الأخيرة يبرز فيها النظر إلى المصالح المرسلة في صورة الاستحسان المخالف لمقتضى القياس (ر: ف ٩/٥)
ومن هنا يلوح لنا وجه الاتفاق بين المذهبين الحنفي والمالكي في نظرية الاستصلاح فالفكرة فيهما واحدة ولكن المذهب المالكي لتأخره عن الحنفي في التاريخ تركزت فيه الصياغة الفنية الفقهية لقاعدة المصالح المرسلة وشرائطها فبرزت فيه واشتهر بها.
١٦/٥ - ثم ظهر الاجتهاد الشافعي بإنكار نظريتي الاستحسان والمصالح المرسلة بحجة أن الشريعة الإسلامية قد تكفلت ببيان كل ما يحتاج الإنسان إلى معرفته من الأحكام إما بالنص الصريح، أو بالإشارة، أو بطريق القياس المشروع، وأنَّ الاستحسان لا ضابط له ولا مقاييس يقاس بها الحق بالباطل، فلو جاز لكل مفت أو حاكم أو مجتهد أن يستحسن لأصبح الأمر فُرُطاً .
وقد عقد الإمام الشافعي في كتاب (الأم) بحثاً خاصاً سماه «كتاب إبطال الاستحسان» (۱) .

وكذلك عد الغزالي الشافعي في كتابه المستصفى» الاستحسان والاستصلاح من الأدلة الموهومة، ويعتبرها من قبيل الحكم بالتشهي والهوى المجرد عن دليل شرعي (المستصفى ١٣٧/١ - ١٤٤ طبعة المطبعة التجارية سنة / ١٣٥٦ هـ).

١ - الملاءمة
بین المصلحة الملحوظة ومقاصد الشرع بحيث لا تنافي أصلاً من أصوله ولا دليلاً من أدلته القطعية، بل تكون من جنس المصالح الكلية التي قصد الشارع إلى تحصيلها وإن لم يشهد دليل خاص باعتبارها .
Chapter 5: The Subordinate Sources – Istislah (Consideration of Public Interest). Qiyasi rules when maslahah requires contravening those rules to avoid a problem that qiyas would lead to, as mentioned previously. Meanwhile, the concept of masalih in the Hanafi ijtihad method is considered a branch of istihsan, because the Hanafis took the istihsan of necessity, which contravenes the dictates of qiyasi rules, as a starting point that necessarily leads to adopting masalih mursalah that do not contradict any rules. It is well-known about Maliki ijtihad that it considers masalih mursalah as an independent source whose consideration is indicated by the texts of Shari'ah just as qiyas is indicated. So on its basis, legal rulings are established when there is no legislative text in the incident or in what resembles it. Thus, masalih become the proof when there is no proof other than it. Likewise, it contravenes qiyas based on a text in similar incidents when the consistency of qiyas leads to contradicting maslahah. This last case shows the consideration of masalih mursalah in the form of istihsan that contravenes the dictates of qiyas (see: 9/5). From here, the point of agreement between the Hanafi and Maliki schools in the theory of istislah becomes apparent; the idea in both is one. However, the Maliki school, being later in history than the Hanafi, saw the technical fiqh formulation of the principle of masalih mursalah and its conditions concentrated in it, so it emerged and became famous. 16/5 – Then Shafi'i ijtihad appeared, denying the theories of istihsan and masalih mursalah on the grounds that Islamic Shari'ah has undertaken to explain everything that a person needs to know of rulings, either by explicit text, by indication, or by the prescribed qiyas; and that istihsan has no control or standards by which truth is distinguished from falsehood. If it were permissible for every mufti, ruler, or mujtahid to exercise istihsan, the matter would become excessive. Imam al-Shafi'i, in his book al-Umm, devoted a special treatise called 'Kitab Ibtal al-Istihsan' (The Book of Refuting Istihsan). He says, as reported from him: 'It is not for a mujtahid to legislate; whoever exercises istihsan has legislated.' That is, the duty of a mujtahid is to determine the rulings of Shari'ah, not to legislate from himself. If he resorts to istihsan, he has taken on legislation. This, in our view, is an exaggeration from Imam al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him). Indeed, those mujtahids who resorted to istihsan and istislah only followed a path opened for them by Shari'ah, indicated to them by the evidences of its texts, principles, and objectives. Similarly, al-Ghazali, a Shafi'i, in his book al-Mustasfa, considered istihsan and istislah among the illusory proofs, and considered them as judgments based on mere desire and whim devoid of any legal evidence (al-Mustasfa 1/137-144, commercial press edition, 1356 AH). However, he did not mean by that to include qiyasi istihsan according to the Hanafis and rulings of compelling necessities; rather, he affirmed that because there is no room for doubt in it, but he referred it back to qiyas. Note 1: It should be noted that istihsan in al-Shafi'i's usage includes what in fiqh terminology is called masalih mursalah and istihsan according to the Hanafis and Malikis (see: book 'Malik' by Professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra, section two, p. 188, footnote, and his book 'al-Shafi'i' also, p. 121, first edition). Note 2: In response to the Shafi'i view that istihsan has no control and is a statement based on mere whim devoid of evidence, Professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra (may Allah have mercy on him) says in his book 'Malik,' quoting from al-I'tisam by Imam al-Shatibi al-Maliki, author of al-Muwafaqat: 'Indeed, scholars of the Maliki school who are familiar with its methods of deduction have observed that its extensive adoption of masalih mursalah was directed toward restrictions that ward off its dangers, so that the assessment of maslahah is not subject to whims and desires. Those restrictions are: 1 – Compatibility between the observed maslahah and the objectives of Shari'ah, such that it does not contradict any of its principles or any of its definitive proofs, but rather is of the kind of universal masalih that the Lawgiver intended to achieve, even if no specific evidence indicates its consideration.'
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a comparative legal philosophy discussion on the subordinate source of istislah (public interest) in the Hanafi, Maliki, and Shafi'i schools. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that in the Hanafi method, the concept of maslahah is a branch of istihsan, since necessity-based istihsan leads to adopting masalih mursalah that do not contradict existing rules. In contrast, the Maliki school treats masalih mursalah as an independent source, used when no explicit text or analogy is found, and can even override qiyas when performing qiyas would harm public interest. The author notes that despite differences in formulation, the Hanafi and Maliki schools agree in substance; the Maliki school simply articulated the principle more systematically later. Then the Shafi'i school, represented by Imam al-Shafi'i and later al-Ghazali, rejected both istihsan and masalih mursalah, arguing that Shari'ah is comprehensive and that istihsan lacks objective criteria, leading to arbitrary legislation. Al-Shafi'i's famous saying 'Whoever practices istihsan has legislated' charges that istihsan usurps the role of the Lawgiver. Al-Zarqa criticizes this as excessive, asserting that those who used istihsan were guided by Shari'ah's own indications and objectives. The passage further clarifies that al-Ghazali did not reject qiyasi istihsan (Hanafi) or rulings of necessity; he acknowledged them but reclassified them as qiyas. Finally, the author quotes Muhammad Abu Zahra, citing al-Shatibi, to demonstrate that Maliki practice of masalih mursalah is not arbitrary; it is constrained by conditions, especially that the maslahah must be compatible with the universal objectives of Shari'ah and not contradict any definitive text or principle.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference whereby a ruling departs from qiyas in favor of a more suitable outcome due to necessity, custom, or public interest.
مصالح مرسلة (masalih mursalah): Consideration of public interest not supported by specific text but in line with the general objectives of Shari'ah.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical deduction from a textual ruling to a new case based on shared effective cause (illa).
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity that permits deviation from a standard ruling to avoid hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the Hanafi madhhab regard masalih mursalah?
A) As an independent source equal to qiyas.
B) As a branch of istihsan derived from necessity.
C) As completely invalid and rejected.
D) As the primary source overriding all others.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi and Maliki schools accept istihsan and masalih mursalah as valid sources constrained by Shari'ah objectives, while the Shafi'i school rejects them as lacking objective criteria, though even al-Ghazali conceded necessity-based exceptions.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Istislah Across the Schools: Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali, and Tufi
Iftaa/usul: Analysis of istislah across madhahib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali, and Tufi positions on istislah, apply the Maliki constraints to evaluate a claim of maslaha, and identify the mufta bihi approach for a mufti.

Core Idea
Istislah is constrained maslaha: Shafi'is reject it, Malikis accept it with three safeguards, Hanbalis subsume it under qiyas.

The validity of considering public interest (maslaha) not directly supported by specific texts is a major point of ikhtilaf in usul al-fiqh. In this lesson, we examine the sharp critique of istislah by al-Shafi'i and al-Ghazali, and the responses from the Maliki and Hanbali schools. For a mufti today, understanding these positions is essential when weighing public benefit against textual evidence, especially in novel cases (nawazil).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Is istislah (masalih mursala) a valid source for deriving legal rulings? Identify the main schools and their contrasting stances.
The dispute centers on whether rulings can be based on a maslaha not explicitly recognized by revelation.
2
State the Shafi'i objection
Recall al-Shafi'i's statement: 'Whoever uses istihsan has legislated.' Understand that he rejects both istihsan and istislah as forms of legislation without divine authority.
Al-Shafi'i's objection is foundational to the opposition against istislah.
3
Analyze al-Ghazali's exception
Note that al-Ghazali excludes Hanafi qiyasi istihsan and rules of compelling necessity from his critique of istislah, accepting them as valid but re-characterizing them as qiyas.
This shows that even within the Shafi'i school, there is nuance.
4
Examine Maliki constraints from al-Shatibi
The Maliki school, as quoted from al-Shatibi, imposes three restrictions on maslaha: (1) compatibility with Shari'ah objectives, (2) rationality, (3) removal of hardship. Work through each.
These constraints ensure maslaha is not arbitrary.
5
Contrast Hanbali view and Tufi's extreme position
The Hanbalis treat masalih mursala as a type of qiyas, not an independent source. Al-Tufi's view that maslaha overrides even definitive texts is condemned as dangerous.
Tufi's position is rejected by the mainstream.

Chapter Two: Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence

Invalidation of Istihsan (1).
Rulings
And al-Shafi'i says, according to what is transmitted from him: 'The mujtahid is not to legislate, and whoever uses istihsan has legislated.' That is, the task of the mujtahid is to determine the rulings of the Shari'ah, not to legislate from himself. So if he resorts to istihsan, he has undertaken legislation. And this from Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, is in our view an exaggeration. For those mujtahids who resorted to istihsan and istislah only followed a path opened for them by the Shari'ah and indicated to them by the evidences of its texts, its principles, and its objectives.

Similarly, al-Ghazali al-Shafi'i in his book al-Mustasfa counted istihsan and istislah among the illusory evidences, and considers them as akin to ruling by whim and caprice devoid of legal evidence. But by that he did not intend to include the qiyasi istihsan of the Hanafis and the rulings of compelling necessities; rather he affirmed that, as there is no room for doubt in it, but he referred it back to qiyas.

(1) It is noted that istihsan in al-Shafi'i's usage includes what is called in juristic terminology al-masalih al-mursala and istihsan according to the Hanafis and Malikis (ref: Kitab Malik by Shaykh Muhammad Abu Zahra, part two, p. 188, footnote, and his book al-Shafi'i also, p. 121, first edition). (2) In response to the Shafi'i view that istihsan has no control and that it is a statement of mere caprice devoid of evidence, Shaykh Muhammad Abu Zahra says in his book Malik, quoting from al-I'tisam by Imam al-Shatibi al-Maliki, author of al-Muwafaqat: 'Scholars of the Maliki school who are familiar with its methods of deduction have observed that the extensiveness in adopting al-masalih al-mursala was directed towards restrictions that eliminate its dangers so that the estimation of maslaha is not subject to whims and desires. Those restrictions are: 1. Compatibility between the observed maslaha and the objectives of the Shari'ah, such that it does not contradict any of its principles or any of its definitive evidences, but rather it is of the type of universal interests that the Lawgiver intended to achieve, even if no specific evidence attests to its consideration.'

PAGE 121
Chapter (5) Secondary Sources: Istislah

5/17 - Then came Hanbali ijtihad, which took the direction of Maliki ijtihad in considering masalih as a principle to rely upon in determining rulings. According to this ijtihad, the jurist can rule that every act that has a dominant maslaha is considered legally required without needing a specific evidence from the texts of the Shari'ah for this type; and every matter whose harm and sin is greater than its benefit is prohibited without needing a specific text. However, the Hanbalis in reality do not consider al-masalih al-mursala as an independent source in itself as the Malikis consider it, rather the Hanbalis see it as a type of qiyas, subordinate to it, and in its rank as consideration.

18/5 - And Sulayman al-Tufi exaggerated in considering maslaha, making it precede the definitive texts if they conflict with it. This is a very dangerous opinion that leads to suspending legislative texts by a purely ijtihadi view. And if it were permissible for the nation to accept...
= 2. That it be rational in itself such that when presented to people of intellect they accept it. 3. That adopting it removes hardship in religion, such that if that rational maslaha in its situation were not adopted, people would be in hardship, and Allah Almighty says: 'And He has not placed upon you in religion any hardship' (Qur'an 22:78).
(1) This was narrated by the researcher Shaykh Muhammad Abu Zahra in his book Malik which he published in 1365 AH, and he counted al-Tufi among the Hanbalis. When Shaykh Abu Zahra published his book Ibn Hanbal, he verified that al-Tufi was a Shi'ite, saying in that what the Imami Shi'ah say. But al-Sayyid Abd al-Husayn Sharaf al-Din, one of the Imami Shi'ah scholars in Jabal Amil, wrote an article in al-Irfan magazine (issue Rajab 1373 AH) absolving the Imami school from the view of al-Tufi and counting him among the extremists. After that, Dr. Mustafa Zayd from Dar al-Ulum published his valuable dissertation on al-masalih al-mursala, in which he verified with precise historical and transmission-based evidence that al-Tufi was not a Shi'ite, but rather his opponents accused him of that out of envy, and that he was of the Hanbali school but free-thinking.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the secondary sources istihsan and istislah, focusing on the objections raised by al-Shafi'i and al-Ghazali and the responses from the Maliki and Hanbali schools. The author (Mustafa al-Zarqa) begins by presenting al-Shafi'i's famous statement that 'whoever uses istihsan has legislated,' implying that istihsan is an illegitimate departure from textual evidence. Al-Zarqa regards this as an exaggeration, arguing that those who use istihsan and istislah are following a path opened by the Shari'ah itself. He then notes that al-Ghazali considered istihsan and istislah as 'illusory evidences,' but explicitly excluded Hanafi qiyasi istihsan and rules of necessity, which he referred back to qiyas. The passage then shifts to the Maliki school, quoting restrictions from al-Shatibi that ensure maslaha is not based on whims: compatibility with Shari'ah objectives, rationality, and removal of hardship. For the Hanbali school, the author explains that while they use masalih mursala extensively, they do not consider it an independent source; rather, it is a type of qiyas. Finally, al-Tufi's extreme view that maslaha can override definitive texts is condemned as dangerous, and a scholarly controversy about his madhhab (whether he was Hanbali or Shi'i) is recounted. The overall lesson is that while istislah is accepted by some schools, it is always constrained by the principles of the Shari'ah.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; an exception to qiyas jali based on a stronger evidence or public interest.
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): Considering public interest (maslaha) not attested by specific scriptural evidence as a basis for ruling.
مصالح مرسلة (masalih mursala): Public interests for which no specific textual evidence exists but which are not rejected by the Shari'ah.
الملاءمة (al-mula'ama): Compatibility of the maslaha with the objectives of the Shari'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do the Hanbalis view al-masalih al-mursala?
A) As an independent source of law alongside the Qur'an and Sunnah.
B) As a type of qiyas, subordinate to it and in its rank.
C) As invalid and never used in deriving rulings.
D) As a source that overrides definitive texts when maslaha is strong.

Key Takeaway
Istislah is not an arbitrary tool; it is governed by compatibility with Shari'ah objectives, rationality, and removal of hardship.
